REPORTED CAPTURE OF
COLENSO BY THE BOERS
PARIS, Nov. 3.â The Liberte says Boers in strong force under General Lucas Meyer are occupying
the town of Colenso, twenty miles south of Ladysmith, thus completely investing the latter place.
General Meyer, according to the correspondent, now holds the railway from Ladysmith to Picter
maritzburg and Durban. The correspondent adds that General Meyer took part in Monday's battle,
but continued his flanking movement until he reached Colenso. The result was renewed fighting south
of Ladysmith, in which the British lost another 1000 in killed, wounded an i prisoners
BRUSSELS, Nov. 3. â It is reported here that General Lucas Meyer has defeated General White
south of Ladysin tli and captured Colenso. The British commander was wounded. A force of Orange
Free State Boers has occupied Colesburg, on the Midland Railway, eleven and a half miles south of the
Free State border.

Before Carleton's Column
Was Compelled to Surrender.
LONDON. Nov. 2 â General Sir George Stewart White has cabled the War
Office that In the engagement on Farquhar's farm, near Lariysmith, Octo
ber 30, when Lieutenant Colonel Carleton's column was compelled to sur
render, six officers were killed and nine wounded.
Among the non-commissioned officers and men the casualties were 54
killed and 231 wounded.
General White promises a list of the missing later.

LORD LANSDOWNE
TALKS OF THE WAR
LONDON, Nov. 2.â The Marquis of
Lansdowne. Secretary of State for War,
was the chief speaker at the Cutlers'
feast in Sheffield this evening, the func
tion being attended by the usual distin
guished assemblage. Replying to a toast,
"Hher Majesty's Ministers." he went over
the usual ground in explaining the cause
of the war and defended the Government
against the charge that the military
preparations were not abreast of the ne
gotiations. He said:
"The diplomatic messages went by wire,
tht reinforcements by ship. Moreover, in
took a leisurely Btroll from Allenhurst to
Long Branch, along the ocean front, and
return. He then loafed around for a
while, joking with his trainers, and at
half-past 4 o'clock bade good-by to his
acquaintances and boarded the train,
leaving at that hour for New York.
Although Sharkey had planned to come
to the city to-night, he changed his mind
at the last moment and decided to remain
at his training quarters at Newdorp
until to-morrow morning, when he will
leave for the battleground.
Sharkey said that he did not think Jef
fries could take a good hard punch suc
cessfully. He said that in the only really
j difficult fight the Californian has had â
that with Fltzsimmonsâ he received only
j a few hard blows, owing to Fitzsimmons'
â  wild delivery. Somebody called Sharkey's
I attention to the fact that Jeffries had an
extremely long reach and asked If Fitz
simmons failed to get inside the big
Californian's guard, how could he (Shar
key), a smaller man, hope for better re
sults. Sharkey pointed out that he had
never yet failed to get inside the guard of
any man he had ever fought. He said
that he had successfully got past the
guards of such clever fighters as McCoy,
Corbett and Fitzsimmnns, and not only
succeoiloil In hitting them, but landed with
sufficient force to score a knockdown in
each instance.
Sharkey not only believes that he is a
much stronger man than Jeffries, but that
he can deliver a blow with much greater
force than the champion.
A comparison of the style of fighting of
the two men is interesting. Jeffries, who
is acknowledged to be the more scientific
fighter of the pair, assumes a low,
crouching position when in action. He al
ways keeps his left on guard, ready to
meet an opponent with a left hook or a
straight jab. His low position makes it
easy for him to move his head out of the
way of swinging blows. His footwork is
also excellent, in fact remarkable for a
man of his physique and weight. He is as
nimble on his pedal extremities as a light
weight. He hits straight from the shoul
der, and when he lands squarely generally
staggers or sends his opponent to the
boards. Jeffries' ability in this direction
was clearly demonstrated in his fight with
Fitzsimmons, from whom he won his pres
ent title of heavy-weight champion pu
gilist of the world.
Sharkey's style of fighting is very unlike
that of Jeffries'. The sailor is by no means
as finished a boxer as the Californlan. He
is, however, fairly clever, but in the heat
and excitement of battle usually forgets
hi# knowledge of the finer noints of box
ing. He is an aggressive, rough and ready,
"get there" sort of fighter. He keeps af
ter his man while swinging his right and |
left, and is not discouraged if he receives
a hard blow or fails to land. He will take
two or three blows in order to land one.
In point of physical perfection, no two
better pugilists ever fought for the heavy
weight championship. In former intes-ts
the question of dissipation and youth
against age has figured conspicuously, but
in to-morrow nlght'a battle honors are

Lord Lansdowne explained the
promptness of the mobilization which
had enabled five cavalry regiments, ten
batteries and thirty battalions to get far
away on the sea. During the earlier ope
rations the enemy, he said, had great su
periority of numbers and choice of
ground. This phase of the situation, he
hoped, was approaching its conclusion,
for in less than a week the first install
ments of Sir Redvers Buller's force would
arrive at Cape Town, to be followed at a
steady rate by the iemainder.
"I do not think," continued the Secre
tary of State for War. "that there need
be any anxiety for the result. There has
been nothing in history to compare with
the patriotism of our colonies, who will
take no denial. Their attitudes will im
prtss on the civilized w*rM two great
truths-first, that Great Britain is not an
empty phrase, and second, that such a
large measure of voluntary support would
not have been accorded unless we were
fighting In a just cause."
In conclusion. Lord Lansdowne ex
pressed, on behalf of the Government
"profound admiration for the heroism of
our troops, who have rehabilitated the
reputation of the British soldier in South
Africa-a great gain, which to my mind
outweighs the heavy losses we have sus
tained, and outweighs even the sad dis
aster which was due to an accidental
cause."
Rear Admiral Lord Charles Beresford
replying to the toast, "The Imperial
Forces," praised the transport arrange
ments. Referring to the surrender of the
Irish Fusileers and the Gloucestershire
Regiment after their ammunition was ex
pended, he said it was an honorable sur
render. He expressed the opinion that the
Government was not sending men enough
to South Africa, in view of the probability
that the Cape Dutch would join the Boers.
Discussing the general question of mili
tary arrangements, he observed that there
were only two really patriotic nations in
the worldâ the British and the Americans
âbecause they alone had volunteer
armies.
THAT MULE STAMPEDE.
LONDON. Nov. 3.â A dispatch to the
Daily Telegraph from Ladysmith, filed
Monday night, giving a description of the
battle says:
"A similar mule stampede occurred to
Lieutenant Colonel Grimwood's column on
the Buluwan side. The ammunition was
lost, but our infantry quite h*ld their
own. It was a serious misfortune that
the Powerful's bluejackets were not sum
moned sooner, as the result of the en
gagement would have been different."
The Daily News has a dispatch this
morning from Ladysmith, dated Tuesday
at 10:30 a. m., which gives the first inde
pendent account of the cutting off of
Lieutenant Colonel Carleton's column In
the engagement at Farquhar's Farm.
The correspondent says:
"The column was sent out Sunday
night, made, a wide detour and reached
the spurs of the Drakensburg before
dawn. Colonel Carleton stormed the
hoights with the bayonet and made his
position against great odds, until his am
munition was exhausted and surrender
had become inevitable. Nearly 200 had
then been killed and wounded."
GERMAN CORPS' APPEAL.
BERLIN. Nov. 2.â The Vossische Zeit
ung publishes an appeal signed by the
committee of the German corps In the
Transvaal calling upon Germans to con
tribute to the support of the families of
"those German volunteers who have
taken up arms in a just cause."
The Hamburgische Correspondenz. in
the course of a semi-official article which
cautions "those who desire to embroil
Germany in a conflict with Great Brit
ain," says:
"Germany is not going to pluck chest
nuts for other people out of Great Brit
ain's fire. Such a policy would drive Ger
many so closely into the arms of Russia
that she would hardly be able to
breathe."

I and over G4 per cent of the whole enlisted

Upon the subject- of receiving-ships
Admiral Crownlnshield points out the
great expense of maintaining such ships
in condition, with the result that after all
they are nothing more than floating
bouses. They should give way. he says,
to permanent barracks on shore. For the
health of the men, for sanitary
and hygienic reasons, for drills and
discipline, for economy of administra
tion . for the proper recreation
of the men and for reduced cost of main
tenance and repair and care-taking, per
manent buildings for the men should be
constructed at the larg_e naval stations.
It is estimated that should the c.angebe
made the Government would save $148,
--600 per annum. The reports show, as the
i result of Admiral CrowninÃhield's in
: spection visit to Europe, that not only
! England, but France, Germany, Italy,
Austria, Russia and Japan have con
structed such barracks. . Therefore,' it Is
proposed to construct barracks at the
New York Navy-yard, at League Island
(for the accommodation of the reserve
crews it !a proposed to. hold for the care
of the ships to be laid up in reserve there),
at Mare Island and at Boston.
Upon the subject of training of lands
men, the report states that the depart
ment was led to undertake this by reason
of a shortage of men to meet the demands
of the navy. It is hoped that in the
future the inflow of apprentices will be
sufficient to meet such needs, but mean
time the department -has been obliged: to
enlist several hundred young men who. it
is proposed to train for five or ten months
on board of training-ships. With .this
object the Hartford has been com
missioned at San Francisco, and with 850
young men will come around Cape Horn
:to the Atlantic. The Lancnstershire will
â  make a similar cruise from New York to
the West Indies, and the steamer Dixie
will take on 400 men from the League
Island yard for the same purpose. It is
calculated In this way to provide tor The
needs of the service for the next two 'or
three years.
The report calls attention to the failure
of the personnel hill as amended to pro
vide officers sufficient to do the work of
the navy. It Is said that there are not
nearly enough officers available to train
the men In gunnery. Had the course at
Annapolis been reduced from six to four
years, as proposed In the original bill,
this fault, the admiral says, would have
I been overcome. It is also submitted, In
conclusion, that the naval pay should he
equalized with that of the army corps,
and that the State militia be federated
into a national naval reserve.
BOLD ATTEMPT
TO ROB A BANK
Robbers Try Twice, but
Fail Each Time.
Special Dispatch to The Cntl.
VANCOUVER. B. C, Nov. The
steamer Miowera from Sydney to-day
brings a sensational story of the attempt
ed robbery from the Bank of New South
Wales In Melbourne of Â£10.000..
Chief Cashier Pearson was temporarily
absent from his desk when two men en
tered the bank, one of them asking the
sub-teller for change for half a crown. As
the teller turned to procure the change he
was confronted with the head and shoul
ders of a second man, who was half over
a wooden barrier with Â£10,000 In his hands.
The teller forced the thief to drop the
money and both robbers hurriedly left the
bank, but had the effrontery to return to
the bank within fifteen minutes and make
mother attempt to capture the money. In
this they were not successful. Two men
named Vergt and Harris have been ar
rested for the attempted theft.

ants will have a hard battle for eight

. Â«
TWO FILIPINO LEADERS RESIGN

â¦
Brilliant Work of Lieutenant Sla-
yens and Scouts; Who Rout a

Enemy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA, Nov. 2.â Agulnaldo has
issued a proclamation announcing that
the American Congress will meet in
December to decide whether the "impe
rialistic policy" and "this bloody work"
are to be continued. He exhorts his sol
diers to conduct themselves co that Con
gress will consider them worthy of inde
pendence, and requests the priests to ab
ptain from politics and to redeem the
church from the bad name the misdeeds
of the friars have given it.
A crisis in the Filipino Cabinet Is pre
dicted as a result of the resignations of
Paterno and Buencamino, two Filipino
leaders who have lost tho confidence of
the rabid revolutionists.
Some Filipinos who attempted to come
to Manila with the Spanish Commission
received a message from Major General
Otis saying the women and children
would be given American protection, but
the men who had cust their lot with the
insurrection must remain with it. The
party included a brother of General Luna,
who was assassinated by the guard before
Aguir.aldo's headquarter.-;, with his fam
ily. ;uid the Filipino Secretary of the
Treasury. The families returned' to Tar
lac.
Lieutenant Slavens nf General MacAr
thur'a staff, recoimoitering with eighteen
wnen In front of Angeles, discovered a Fili
pino outpost in a trench. The Filipinos
numbered about forty men. As the Fili
pinos had sighted the Americans, Lieu
tenant Slavens' only course was to
charge, and his party rushed to the
trench, shooting and yelling. They killed
three of the insurgents and wounded sev
eral, who, however, succeeded in escap
ing. Not one of the Americans was hit.
The lieutenant secured valuable informa
tion about the enemy's position.
Thi- feeling of disquiet aroused at Iloilo
by the arrest of Santiago, the wealthy
Filipino who is charged with forming a
revolutionary Junta, has subsided. The
natives in their Quarter of the town were
BUllen and restless and were heard chant
ing the death song at night, with the re
frain, "The Americans Bleep." A provost
marshal's force Burtounded the quarter
and drove the suspicious-looking natives
outside the lints.
The Americans occupy Iloilo and the
adjoining towns of Jaro and ftfolo with
: en, consisting of the Eighteenth and
Twenty-sixth regiments, two battalions
of the Nineteenth regiment, a â¢ i.;-o'h
ment of the Sixth regiment and a battery
of the Sixth artillery.
The Insurgent force is now supposed to
be between 2000 and 6000 armed men and
many more unarmed. Their lines are
about 900 yards from Jaro, which is oc
cupied by the Twenty-sixth regiment.
The insurgents are reported to have five
smooth-bore cannon. For a long time
past they have been building trenches be
tween Jaro and their stronghold, Santa
Barbara, eight miles north.

SHOT THE WOMAN
WHO REJECTED HIM
George M. Hubbard Attempts to
Commit Murder and Then
Blows Out His Brains.
LOS ANGELES, Nov. 2.â George M.
Hubbard, a prominent real estate dealer
of Colton, this evening shot and seriously
wounded Edna Howard and then fired a
bullet into his own brain. He died in
stantly. The shooting took place in a
lodging-house kept by a Mrs. Cunning
ham, in an alley off Fifth street, and was
the result of Hubbard' s infatuation for
the woman while he was under the influ
ence of liquor and lack of encouragement
on her part
Hubbard hns a wife and four children in
< ..It. m, but he frequently visited this city
and consorted with the Howard woman.
He came here about a week ago and has
been drinking heavily ever since. Early
this evening he visited Edna Howard and
she tried to prevail upon him to return to
his wife and family. He started to leave
the house, but turned suddenly ami shot
her in the right shoulder blade, inflicting
a serious wound. He then ended his own
life with a bullet in the brain.
FERRY-BOAT CRASHES
INTO THE PATTERSON
Revenue Cutter Was Badly Damaged.
but Fortunately No One Was
Seriously Injured.
SEATTLE. Nov. 2.â The local ferry City
of Seattle ran down the cutter Patterson
at her anchorage this morning. The long
overhang of the ferryboat crashed into
the side of the ship, knocking the officers
out of their bunks and clear across the
wardroom. A hole was made Inside the
cutter fifteen feet wide and eight feet
high just above the water line. The ferry
boat was not damaged. Nobody was seri
ously injured.
ENGLAND WILL GET
THE SAMOAN ISLANDS
Germany Accepts the Gilbert and
Solomon Islands in Exchange
for Her Interests in Samoa.
LONDON, Nov. 3.â A dispatch to the
Telegraph from Berlin says it is now cer
t.iin the Samoan Islands wih become Brit
ish territory, Germany having consented
to ac<je.pt the Gilbert and Solomon islands
in exchange for her interests in Samoa.
She will thus henceforth own all the
groups comprised In Micronesia except
Guam.
CAPTAIN GREEN DEAD.
Was One of the Earliest Pioneers of
the State.
SONOMA, Nov. 2.â Captain William
Green, Sonoma Valley's oldest resident.
is dead. He passed away at his Embarca
dero home, where he had lived for forty
seven years, at 1 o'clock this afternoon,
surrounded by his aged wife, sons and
daughters. Captain Green came to Cali
fornia with the celebrated Colonel Ste
venson regiment in 1847. In 1852 he located
at Exnbarcaderq, four miles from Sonoma,
where be has resided continuously ever
since. Deceased leaves a widow, two sons
and two daughters, the latter being Mrs.
C.*H. Wise and Mrs. Carrie Lund. L. H.
Green and William Green Jr. are the eons,
the former being a prominent young busi
ness man of this place. Captain Green
was born in New York City, and was
aged 78 years.
POLITICAL.
CITIZENS 5
NON-PARTISAN
PARTY.
FOR GOOD GOVERNMENT.
To the Voters of San Francisco: The Non-
PartlFan party was organized Beven years ago
to wrest from professional politicians the con-
trol of municipal affairs and to separate en-
tirely from national politics the selection of
city and county officials.
It has at all times opposed machine politics,
and soukM to elect worthy men to ofnee toy in-
dependent nomination or indorsement.
The last Legislature passed a law expressly
aimed at the "unwelcome interference in
1 puhllc affairs of non-partisan organizations.
! The Rlckard act forbids any Information on the
i ballot concerning the Indorsements that a can-
! dldate may receive and makes It impossible for
â  a man tn"be nominated by two parties such
I a creditable occurrence as the nomination last
I year by the two great parties of John Lack-
! Mann "for Supervisor could not be repeated,
i We regard this law as pernicious in principle
I and against public policy, and we intend to
; vigorously contest It.
i the adoption of a non-partisan charter, tne
i result of the last primary election, and the in-
â  dependent character of the recent Republican
i and Democratic conventions, clearly Indicate,
however, that the friends of good government
i have awakened to their duties. ...,.*
With these changed conditions we feel that
there is no occasion for an entire Non-Partisan
ticket at this election.
It is however, a fundamental principle of
this party to support those nominees who have
faithfully and fearlessly filled their offices and
are r?nominated for the same office. "S\ c would
fall in our obligations were we to leave this
I work undone. *
We therefore unqualifiedly recommend for
your Suffrage at the coming election:
For Mayor JAMES D. PHELAN
For Assessor. WASHINGTON DODGE
For Auditor ASA R, WELLS
! For City and County Attorney-..
FRANKLIN K. LANE
To each of these faithful servants of the
people there Is certainly due a generous meai-
ure of credit and encouragement, for the able
and h.mest manner In which they have ful-
i filled their public trust.
i This committee, furthermore, realizing the
: difficulty for each Individual voter to inform
himself as to the qualifications of the many
I candidates for the Board of Supervisors, has

DECLARES THE FILIPINOS ARE
INCAPABLE OF SELF-GOVERNMENT
Commission Submits a Preliminary Report to the President
Stating That No Promises Were Made to Aguinaldo
and Praising the Work of General Otis.
WASHINGTON, Nov. 2.â ln ac
cordance with the understand
ing reached at the conference
at the White House yesterday
the Philippine Commission sub
mitted to the President the preliminary j
i report which It had promised to prepare, j
The report appears to be a compact i
summary of conditions on the Islands a3
j the commission left them; of the histori
cal events which preceded the Spanish
war and led to the original Filipino in
surrection; of the exchanges between Ad-
I mlral Dewey and the other American
commanders and the insurgents, and the
i breaking out and the progress of the
I present insurrection, and finally a state-
I ment of the capacity of the Filipinos for
self-government. A notable feature of the
j report Is a memorandum by Admiral
I Dewey explanatory of his relations with
j Agulnaldo.
The commission tells briefly how It con
ducted the task entrusted to It. hearing
â¢ statements from all classes of people In
Manila as to the capabilities of the Fili
pinos for self-government, the habits and
customs of the people and also the estab
lishment of municipal governments in
many towns. All this matter is to be
included In the final report.
Turning to the history of the islands
the commission attaches little importance
to the divers rebellions which had pre
ceded that of IS%. As to this movement
i hey declare that it was in no sense an
attempt to win independence, but solely
to obtain relief from intolerable abuses.
To sustain this statement they quote
from an Insurgent proclamation, showing
, that what was demanded was the ex
pulsion of the friars and the restitution
to the people of their lands, with a di
vision of the episcopal sees between
Spanish and native priests, it was also
demanded that the Filipinos have par
liamentary representation, rreedum
press, religious toleration, economic au
tonomy and laws similar to those of
Spain. The abolition of the power of
banishment was demanded, witn a legal
equality for all persons in law and equal
ity In pay between Spanish and native
civil servants. The commission declares
> that these uemands had good ground;
that on paper the Spanish system of gov
ernment was tolerable, but in practice
â¢ every Spanish governor did what h<_- saw
1 lit and the evil deeds of men In the gov
ernment were bidden from Spain by strict
presd censorship.
Allusion is made to the powerful K^iti
punan Society, patterned on the Masonic
order, and mainly made up ot Tagalos, as
powerful revolutionary force.
Ihe war begun In Lsyti was terminated
;by the treaty of Biac-Na-Bate. The Fili
pinos were numerous, but possessed only
about &*) smallarms. The Spanish f.-it. that
it wuuld require 100,000 men to capture
their stronghold, a-nd concluded to resort
to the us' 3of money. Certain conces
were also decided upon, including n pre
sentation of the Filipinos in the Cortes,
the deportation of the friars, which was
the principal question; the grant of the
right of association and of a frea press.
Governor General Rivera wat; willing to
PÂ«Ly $2,000,000 In Mexican money when
AguinaJdo and his Cabinet and leading
officers arrived in Hongkong. It appears,
however, that Paterno only offered the
latter $400,000, $200,000 to be paid when j
Aguinaldo arrived at Hongkong and the
! balance when the Filipinos had delivered
Jup their arms. The arrangement was not
acceptable to the people.
The promises were never carried out
and Spanish abuse's began afresh in Ma
nila, at one time more than 200 men being
executed. Hence sporadic revolutions oc
curred, though they possessed nothing
Ilk* the strength of the original move
| ment. The insurgents lacked arms, am
munition and leaders. The treaty had
ended the war, which, with the exception
of an unimportant outbreak, has been
confined to Luzon, Spain's sovereignty in
the islands never having been questioned
and the thought of Independence never
having been entertained.
The report then tells how General Au
gustine went to Manila as Governor Gen
! e-ral at this Juncture, and when war broke
; out between Spain and the United States
j Augustine sought to secure the support
i of the Filipinos to defend Spain against
I America, promising them autonomy, but
j the Filipinos did not trust him. Then
: came the Ist of May and the destruction
j of the Spanish fleet by Dewey, with the
resulting loss of prestige to Spain. Then
in June Aguinaldo came. On thi3 point
the commission says:
The following memorandum on this Fuhject
; has bean furnished tho commission by Admiral
i Dewey:
I Memorandum of relations with Agrulnaldo.
! On April U. 1698, the following cipher dispatch
was received at Hongkong: from E. Spencar
: Pratt. United Btates Consul General at Singa
pore: "Agulnaldo. Insurgent leader, here.
Will come to Hongkong, arrange with commo
dore for general co-operation Insurgents Manila
' If deetred. Telegraph.â l'ratt."
; On the tamo day Commodore Dewey tele
graphed Mr. Pratt: "Teli Agulnaldo come as
coon as possible," the necessity for haste being
' due to the fact that the squadron had been
notified by the Hongkong Government to leave
the waters by the following day. The squadron
! left Hongkong on the morning of the 2r,th, and
1 Mlrs Bay on the 2.th. Apulnaldo did not leHve

It had been reported to the commodore as |

| broken out In Insurrection against the Spanish
authority In the vicinity of Manila, and on

j thousand rebels armed In camp near city. Ixiyal
to us In case of war."
Vpon the arrival of the squadron at Manila
| it was found that there was no Insurrection to
speak of, and it wns accordingly decided to al
low Agulnaldo to come to Cavite on board the
MoOuliorh. He arrived with thirteen of his
staff on May 19 and Immediately came on board
the Olympla to call on the commander-ln-hlef.
â  after which he was allowed to land at Cavite '
! and organize an army. This was done with the
; purpose of strengthening the United States
: forces and weakening those of the enemy. No
I alliance of any kind was entered Into with
i Agulnaldo, nor was any promise of independ
, ence made to him. then or at any other time.
I The commission's report then rapidly
i sketches events now historical. It tells In
substance how the Filipinos attacked the
Spanish and how General Anderson ar
â  rived and Agiilnaldo, at his request, re
' moved from Cavite to Bakoor. Saya the
; commission: "Now for the first time rose
the idea of national independence. Aguin
aldo issued a proclamation in which he
took the responsibility of promising It to
his people on behalf of the American Qov
i ernment, although he admitted freely in
private conversation with members ot his
; Cabinet that neither Admiral Dewey nor
; any other American had made him any
j such promise."
The report states that Aguinaldo wished
to attack the Americans when they landed
at Paranaque, but was deterred by lack
of arms and ammunition. From that point
"ii there wad a growing friction between
the Filipinos and the Amt-rk-an troops.
"There was no conference," says the re
: port, "between the officers of the Filipinos
and our officers with a view to operating
* against the Spaniards, nor was there co
operation of any kind. â¢ â¢ â¢ There
[ never was any preconcerted operations or
: any combined movement by the United
! States and Filipinos agralnst the Span
' iard."
Reference is made to Agulnaldo's de
mand that he be allowed to loot Manila
; and take the arms of the Spaniards. The
latter demand is said to confirm the state
; ment that he intended to get possession of
[ the arms to attack the Americans. Fur
ther evidence of the hostile Intentions of
the Filipinos was found in the organlza
| tion of "popular clubs." which later on
j furnished a local militia to attack the
! Americans. The decrees of the Filipino
Congress are also cited, as well as the
making- of bolls (knives) In every shop in
Manila. It is Bhown that a consid
â¢ erable element in the Filipino Con
gress wished to address to President Mc-
Kinley a request not to abandon the Phil
ippines iat this stage the Paris conference
was discussing the future of the Philip
: pines). The President was also to be
: asked his desire as to tho. form of govern
: ment he wished to establish. But all this
I time Aguinaldo was preparing fur war
I and delaying these messages, and it was
understood that the attack would come
i on the lirst act by the American forces
| which would afford a pretext.
A brief chapter tells of the lack of BUC
a attending the effort made at this
i time by General Merrltt, through a com
mission, to arrive at a mutual under
standing with Agulnaldo, as to the inten
tions, purposes and desires of the Filipino
people. This brings th>- story up to the
outbreak on the evening of the 4th of
February, with the attack upon the
j American troops following the action of
j the Nebraskan sentinel. The commissicni,
! in concluding this chapter, says:
After the landing of our troops Agulnaldo
made up his mind that It would be neo*;Â«i<ary
to flght the Americans, and after the making
\rt the treaty of peace at Paris this determina
tion waÂ« strengthened. He did not only openly
dw lare that h<Â» Intended to fight the Americans,
but he excited everybody, and especially the
military, by claiming independence, and It Is
doubtful whether he had the power to check
or control the army at the time hostilities broke
out. I">ei'lorable as war is the one in which
we are now engaged was unavoidable. We
were attacked by a bold, adventurous and en
thusiastic arniy. No alternative was left to us
except Ignominious retreat. It is not to be
conceived that any American could have
sanctioned the surrender of Manila to the In
yurpTits. Our obligations to other nations and
to the friendly Filipinos and to ourselves and
jur flag demand that force should bÂ« met
with force. Whatever the future of the Philip
pines may be thpre Is no course open to us
now except the prosecution of the war until
the Insurgents are rpduc^d to submission. The
commission is of the opinion that there has
been no time since the destruction of the Span.
ish pquadron by Admiral Dewy when It was
possible to withdraw our forces from the
islands either with honor to ourselves or with
safety to the Inhabitants.
The commission then takes the condi
tion of the country at tho time of their
arrival, comparing it with conditions <x-
Istlng at the time they left it a short time
ago. A vivid picture is riven of the nn
archy existing among the inhabitants in
and about Manila during the early spring.
"The situation In the city," says the
commleslpn, "was bad. Incendiary flr>-s
occurred daily. The streets were almost
deserted. Half the native population had
fled and most of the remainder were shut
in their houses. Business was at a stand
still. Insurpc-nt troops everywhere faced
our lines and thf Pound of rifle firing- was
frequently audible in our house. A reign
of terror prevailed. Filipinos who have
favored Americans feared assassination,
and few had the courage to come out
openly for us. Fortunately, therp wore
among this number some or the best men
of the city."
The report then speaks of the issuance
of the commission's proclamation and the
rood -effects It had on public sentiment.
The natives, accustomed to Spanish prom
ises, urged the commission that acts In
stead of promises should be givnn them.
As a result native law courts were estab
lishf-d. and this greatly aided in tho resto
ration of public confidence. The flow of
population soon bogan to set toward tho
city. Natives who had fled from their
homes returned. As showing the limited
scope of the rebellion the commission
states:
Wo learned that the strong anti-American
feeling was confined to the Tagalo provinces,
namely, Manila, f'avlto, Laguna, Batangas,
Morons;, Bulucan, Nueva Eclja, Principe, In
fanta and 7.amba!es. It was strongest In the
first six named ar.rt hardly existed In the lest
four. Thp population of these provinces Is es
timated to be about 1,500,000, but It should not
be supposed that even In the blx provinces Im
mediately adjacent to Manila the people were
united In their opposition to us. Even here
there was a strong conservative element con-
Bistlng of people of wealth and intelligence
opposed to the war.
Under the heading, "The Rebellion Not
a National Movement," the report treats
of the rebellion out of Oio provinces of
Luzon, where It Is stated the uprising was
viewed at first with Indifference and later
with fear. Throughout the archipelago at
large there was trouble only at those
points to which Tagalos had been sent In
considerable numbers. The machinery of
the Insurgent "government" served only
for plundering the people under the pre
text of levying war contributions, while
many of the Insurgent officials were rap
idly "accumulating wealth.
Ap to the state of affairs when the com
mission left, the report says:
Baton the commission left the Philippines
nearly all the Inhabitants have returned to
. ruined villages. Many of the houses bad
li-en rebuilt. Fields that had lain fallow for
three years were green with growing crops.
Municipal governments had been established
and the people, protected by our troops, were
enjoying p*ace. security and a degree of par
ticipation In their own government previously
unknown In the history of the Philippines.
Attempts of the Insurgents to raise recruits
and money In the province of Bulucan were
proving abortive, except when backed by bay
onets and bullets, and even in such cases the
natives were applying to us for help to resist
them.
The chapter devoted to "establishment
of municipal governments" gives In detail
the offorts in that direction. There wore
many difficulties encountered. The condi
tion of the people was found to be most
pitiable. They had been plundered by the
insurgent troops, who had robbed "them
of jewels, money, clothing and even food,
BO that they were literally starving.
Peaceful citizens had been fired on;
women had been maltreated. There was
prnernl satisfaction that the Americans
had come at last and conditions seemed
favorable to the American propaganda.
The towns of Bacoor and Imus were se
lected for the purpose of experiment and
4fter talks with the local "head men," a
local form of g-overnmfnt was established.
Encouraged by thr result, the work was
continued at Paranaque, Las Plnas and
other towns, with similar good results.
At the request of General Lawton, who
had been assigned to this work by Gen
eral Otis, the commission prepared a sim
ple scheme of municipal government,
similar enough to the old system to be
readily comprehensible to the natives, but
giving them liberties which they had
never before enjoyed. This scheme was
adopted and gave general satisfaction. In
every instance enthusiasm ran high be
fore the Commissioners took their depart
ure and cheers were raised for General
Lawton and for the country which he
represented.
The commission states that a large
amount of supervision over the affairs of
our new municipalities proved necessary,
as the officials were timid and slow to
comprehend their new duties. At many
of the elections the voters went about
"asking whom they would vote for," and
it was only with great difficulty that they
were persuaded to exercise the right of
free suffrage.
The commission sums up the situation
at the time of their departure as follows:
AVhen we left Manila a lurgre volume of busi
ness was being done and the streets were so
crowded as to be hardly safe. The native pop
ulation was quiet and orderly and all fear of
an uprising had lonpr since passed. An efficient
corps of native policemen was on duty. A
system of public sehon!.* In which Enitli. J h was
taught had bÂ°en advocated by the commission
an.l established by General Otis. Sume 6000
scholars were In attendance.
In the Tagalo province of I^uzon. where the
anti-American feeling- had been strongest, pub
lic sentiment had greatly changed, aB evi
denced by the fact that the military Governor
of HnlaEana-s had offered to surrender his
troops and his province If we would only Bend
a small force there. The Hlools, In Southern
Luzon, had risen agrainst their Tapalo mas
ters. The Macabebes wore clamoring for an
opportunity to fight In our ranks and native
Â» Idlers and scouts were also serving- under
General Lawton. Stories nf the corruption of
lnsurÃf-nt officers were becoming daily more
common and the disintegration of the enemy's
forces was steadily progressing. The hope of
assistance from outside sources seemed to be
all that held them together.
Having given so much attention to the
Island of Luzon, the commission then
takes in detail the condition of the other
islands. On this point it is stated that
the rebellion Is essentially Tagalo, and
when it ends in Luzon It must end
throughout the archipelago. The situa
tion Â« -lsewhere than Luzon is summed up
as follows:
The only Island, apart frnm Luzon, where
serious trouble threatens Is Panay, to which
a considerate- force of Tagalo soldiers were
sent before an outbreak of hostilities. Many
of the Vtsayana of this Island are opposed to
Tagalos, however, and It Is not believed that
the latter can make a formidable resistance.
In S;itnar, Leyte and Masbate the Tagalo in
vaders are numerically lew and are disliked by
the natives of these islands, whom the- have
oppressed. We were a^ured that 200 men
w.>uld suffice to restore order In Mandoro.
Bobol was asking for troops, the Calamianea
Islanders had sent word that they would wel
come us. There can be no resistance in Pala
wan. Satisfactory relations had already bepn
established with th<> warlike Moros, whose '
Pultan had previously been conciliated by a
member of the commission and in Mindanao,
this tribe had even taken up our cause and
attacked the insurgents, of whom there are
very few in the Island. In Cebu we have only
to reckon with the lawless element, which has
never been very formidable there.
Special attention is given to the island
of Negros, as this seemed a field well
adapted to the extension of an American
system. Here the natives had adopted a
local form of government. Including a
Congress, and had raised the American
flag. They believed themselves capable
of managing their own affairs and asked
for a battalion of troops to hold In check
a mountainous band of fanatics. The bat
talion whs furnished, but the people
proved unable to carry out their pro
gramme, owing to ill feeling among their
own ofilcials. The Americans remained
popular. At the request of General Otis
a new and simplified scheme of govern
ment for the island, giving the people a
large voice in their affairs, but placing an
American in full control, was put into
operation. It brought about satisfaction
and public order is better in the Island to
day than at any time during the last
twenty years. Slimming the failure of
tho native form of government and the
success of the American control, tho com
mission says:
The flat failure of this attempt to establish
an Independent native government In Negros,
conducted as it was under the most favorable
circumstances, makes It apparent that here,
as well as in the less favored provinces, a large
amount of American control Is at iirom-nt abso
lutely essential to a successful administration
of Diibllc affairs.
The efforts at conciliation with Aguln
aldo and his various commissions are set
forth in detail. These commissioners
were assured of the beneficent purposes
of the I'nited States, and the President's
readiness to grant the Filipino r-eople as
large a measure of home rule and as
ample liberty as was consistent with tho
ends of Government, "subject only to the
recognition of the sovereignty of the
United Statesâ a point which " being es
tablished, the commission Invariably re
fused to discuss."
The commission adds that nothing came
of the negotiations, as Agulnaldo's emis
saries were without powers and merely
came and came again for Information.
Courteous reception was accorded to the
insurgent commissions and earnest ap
peals made to stop further bloodshed, ail
witnessing "the spirit of patient concilia
tion" exhibited by the American com
mission In endeavoring to reach an
amicable adjustment with the insurgents
as well as the obduracy of Aguinaldo.
The report sums up the result of these
fruitless exchanges as follows:
No better proof could be furnished that the
primary object of this struggle Is not. as Is
pretended, the liberty of the Filipino peoples,
but the continuance of his own arbltary and
despotic power. In any event, the American
people may feel confident that no effort was
omitted by the commission to secure a peace
ful end of the struggle, but the opportunities
they afforded and urged were all neglected, if
not. Indeed, spurned.
The chapter devoted to "capacity for
self-government" is the result, the report
states, of diligent inquiry for several
months, 'n the course of which a great
number of witnesses were examined of
all shades of political thought and va
rieties of occupation, tribe and locality.
The moat striking, and perhaps ihe most
significant fact In the entire situation is
the multiplicity of tribes inhabiting tlu
archipelago. the diversity of their
languages (which are mutually unintelli
gible) and the multifarious phases of
civilizationâ ranging all the way from the
highest to the lowest. As to this the re
port says: "The Filipinos are not a na
tion, but a variegated assemblage of dif
ferent tribes and peoples, and thtir loyal
ty is still of the tribal type."
Concerning their intellectual capacity,
the commission said:
As tr> the general Intellectual capacities of
the Filipinos the commission Is disposed to rate
them high. But excepting In a limited number
of persons thpse capacities have not been de
veloped by education or experience. The masses
of the people are uneducated. Intelligpnt pub
lic opinion on which popular government rests
does not exist in the Philippines. Ami !t can
not exist until education has elevated the
maÃses. broadened their intellpctun.l horizon
and disciplined their faculty of Judgment. And
even then the power of self-government can
not be assumed without considerable previous
training and experience under the guidance and
tutelage of an enlightened and liberal foreign
power. For the bald fact Is that the Filipinos
have never had any experience in governing
themselves.
The report shows that this inability for
pelf-government is due to the old Spanish
regime, which gave the Filipinos little or
no part In governing themselves. Afier
reviewlng this Spanish system the com
mission sums up on this point:
Tills is all the training in self-government
which the inhabitants of the Philippine Islands
have enjoyed. Their lack of education and po
litical experience, combined with their racial
and linguistic diversities, disqualify them, in
sptte of their mental gifts and democratic vir
tues, to undertake the task of governing the
archipelago at the present time. The most that
can be expected of them is to co-operate with
the Americans in the administration of general
affairs from Manila as a center and to under
take, subject to American control or guidance
(as may be found necessary) the administra
tion of provincial and municipal affairs. For
tunately there are educated Filipinos, though
they do not constitute a large proportion of the
entire population, and their support and ser
vices will be of incalculable value In inaugu
rating and maintaining the new government.
As education advances and experience ripens
the natives may be entrusted with a large
and more Independent share of government,
self-government, as the American Ideal, being
constantly kept in view as the goal. In this
way American sovereignty over the archipelago
will prove a great political boon to the people.
Should our power by any fatality be with
drawn the commission believe the government
of the Philippines would speedily lapse into
anarchy, which would excuse, if It did not
necessitate, the intervention of other powers
and the eventual division of the Islands among
them.
Only through American occupation, therefore.
Is the idea of a free, self-governing and nnlted
Philippine gommonwealth at all conceivable.
And the Indispensable need, from the Filipino
point of view, of maintaining American sover
eignty over the archipelago is recognized by all
Intelligent Filipinos, and even by those Insur
gents who desire an American protectorate.
The latter, it is true, would take the revenues
and leave us the responsibilities. Nevertheless
they .recognize the Indubitable fact that the
Filipinos cannot rtiind alone. Thus the welfare
of the Filipinos coincides with the dictates of
national honor in forbidding our abandonment
of the archipelago. We cannot, from any point
of view, escape the responsibilities of govern
ment which our sovereignty entails, and the
commission Is strongly persuaded that the per
formance of our national duty will prove the
greatest blessing to the peoples of the Philip
pine Islands.
One of the closing chapters of the re
port is devoted to a tribute to
"our soldiers and sailors in the
war." The commission dismisses the
reports of the desecrating of churches,
the murdering of prisoners and
the. committing of unmentionable crimes
and say they are glad to express the be
lief that a war was never more humanely
conducted, saying:
If churches were occupied it was only as a
military necessity and frequently after their
use as forts by tne insurgents had made It
necessary to train our artillery on them. Pris
oners were taken whenever opportunity afford
ed, often to be set at liberty after being dis
armed and fed. Up to the time of our depart
ure, although numerous spies had been cap
tured, not a single Filipino had been exe
cuted.
The commission gives a general view
of the value of the islands, their general
richness in agricultural and forest prod
ucts, their mineral wraith and their com
manding geographical position. They
state that the Philippines should soon
become one of the great traders of the
East. Manila is already connected by
new steamship lines with Australia, India
ami Japan, and she will become the nat
ural terminus of many other lines when
a ship canal connects the Atlantic with
the Pacific. It cannot be doubted that
commerce will greatly increase and the
United States will obtain a large share in
this treatment. The report concludes:
Our control means to the Inhabitants of the
Philippines Internal peace and order, a guar
antee against foreign aggression and against
the dismemberment of their country, commer
cial and industrial prosperity and as large a
share of the affairs of government aa they
shall prove fit to take. When peace and pros
perity shall have been established throughout
the archipelago, when education shall have be
come general, then. In the language of a lead
ing Filipino, his people will, under our guid
ance, "become more Americans than the Amer
icans themsel\-es. "
The report is signed by J. G. Bchur
man, George Dewey, Charles Denby and
Dean C. Worcester.
WILL NOT GIVE
UP A MURDERER
Costa Rica Refuses Wish
of England.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
KINGSTON, Jamaica, Nov. 2.â The
Government of Costa Rica has definitely
refused to surrender Rutherford, the man
charged with the murder of the American
citizen named Archer unless the British
Government guarantees not to inflict cap
ital punishment, to which the Govern
ment cannot accede, thus closing the case
so far as Jamaica is concerned.
Early in 1537 an American named Post
Harding Archer arrived In Jamaica for
the purpose of taking up land and engag
ing In fruit cultivation. 'Jo all appearances
he possessed considerable means both in
ready money and personal effects. Archer
secured a property known as Elervale, in
the parish of Portland, a most valuable
place for his purpose, situated on the very
outskirts of civilization and reached by
a road that led no farther. As a compan
ion and partner in his venture Archer se
cured the services of a Jamaican named
Rutherford.
On October 22, 1897, the body of Archer
was found in an unfrequented spot in a
pool of blood. The body showed two pis
tol shot wounds, one through the brain
and one through the heart. There was no
weapon found, and the flannel undershirt,
shirt and coat were buttoned up and
found to be unperforated by the bullet
that entered the heart.
The body also lay face downward on a
Cile of broken stones, yet the face was not
rulsed. The effects of the deceased were
found to have been stolen.
In spite of these facts the police of the
district, after a delay of a week, set up a
theory of Buicide, with which they went
before the Coroner's jury. The Coroner,
who was the resident magistrate, refused
to allow such a verdict on the evidence
and the recorded, verdict was that "the
deceased came to his death from the ef
fects of two pistol shots, but by whom
discharged does not appear from the evi
dence. The circumstances surrounding
the death of the deceased are so suspi
cious we should suggest that the police
be instructed to Institute further vigilant
investigation."
In Junuary, ISOB, the Government for
mally notified the United States Consul
that "Archer had committed suicide. Mean
while Mr. Elworthy had kept up a lively
correspondence with the Colonial Secre
tary, pointing out the criminal negligence
ami incapacity of the people, but his rep
resentations had no effect.
Early in the present year there was a
shuffling of police, and one result was the
transfer to Portland of one of the shrewd
est and most active inspectors on the
force, Inspector McLeod.
In the interim Mr. Elworthy, a Justice
of the Peace and friend of Archer, had
quietly been carrying on a private inves
tigation with a view to the ultimate clear
ing of the memory of his friend from the i
stain of suicide and to bring the murderer i
to justice. He placed the results in the !
hands of McLeod, and after considering i
them that officer united with Mr. El- !
worthy in urging the Government to order
a reopening of the inquiry.
This was done. Rutherford, who Imme
diately after the holding of the inquest
disappeared from the island, was located
In Costa Rica, where he was arrested on
an extradition warrant from the Jamaican
Government.

STRIKERS' TRIM
NEARING A CLOSE
Prosecution Has Put in
All Evidence.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MOSCOW, Idaho, Nov. 2.â ln the trial
of the Coeur d'Alene miners, for interfer
ing with the mails, the prosecution closed
its case at noon yesterday. During the
proceedings the defense made a motion
to strike out all the testimony by wit
nesses who had testified before the Coro
ner's Jury on the death of Smith and
Chevne the men who were killed on April
29 the day of the alleged obstruction of
the malls. The ground taken was that
the defense had been refused access to
the records of this proceeding, and there
fore the defendants were deprived of the
opportunity to use evidence given prior to
the present testimony for purposes of im
peachment. It was maintained that if
the records were on hand they anticipated
no trouble in showing that some of the
witnesses were false or mistaken in the
statements made in the court. The de
fense introduced ten witnesses who at
tempted to prove alibis for cue prisoners.
To-day many witnesses were introduced
by the defense and the testimony was pre
sented to show that all the prisoners ex
cept one were engaged in other business
and were not present when the mill was
blown up and that they were not a part
of the mob that delayed the mail train.
The defense will put three more wit
nesses on the stand to-morrow and this
will finish the evidence.

ROSINANTE
CAME HOME
ON THE BIT
The Chestnut Mare Displayed
Rare Form â Satsuma
a Bad Ac'Or-
> If her race of yesterday at Oakland is
) any criterion, the dainty Rosinante will
Â» be a strong factor in the purse events and
I | handicaps to be contested for during the
' ! season now in progress. Formerly, she
| ; had the reputation of being a stretch run
â¢ ! tier, but in the mile and a sixteenth sell
| i ing affair the chestnut mare picked up
J ' 104 pounds, and, after making all the pace,
[ ! won easily in 1:47V4, over a slow track. It

DIXON IS
GIVEN THE
DECISION
Splendid, Game Fight With
Curley, the i: nglish
Featherweight.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. Nov. 2.â George Dixon, j
feather-weight champion of the world,
more than held his own to-night against
Will Curley of England, and after fighting
I twenty-live rounds, many of which were
I rather tame, the American was declared
I the winner and no one complained.
Curley is a clever, game boy, but Dixon
is a little bit cleverer and none the less
game. Dixon's victory was well earned
and by his defeat of Curley he re-estab
lishes what has always been claimed, that
he is the superior of Jordan. Palmer and
all Englishmen who have been defeated
by Curley. Curley has no excuse to make
for his defeat to-night other than that he
was outclassed. Dixon was a big favorite,
as much as 100 to 30 being wagered on
him. Before the Englishman donned the
gloves he demanded an assurance that the
purse of $4500 was guaranteed.
In the opening round Curley shot two
hard lefts into Dixon's mouth. Dixon
swung lefts on body and head. There were
light exchanges in the second round, and
in the third Curley had the better of the
fighting, though Dixon was the aggressor
at. the opening. Curley planted a terrific
right straight over Dixon's heart.
Dixon forced matters In the fourth, but
Curley was very clever In dodging his as
saults, ami rurley's blocKlng was almost
perfect.
'The fifth round was Dixon's. He brought
Curley to his tiptoes with a hard left In
the stomach. Curley weakened and Dixon
bored in with a right on* jaw. Curley
clinched, but Dixon fought him off, send
ing In hard ones on the wind and was
forcing Curley all over the ring when the
gong sounded.
Dixon continued his rushing In the
sixth, landing a hard right over the ear.
Curley landed a right on the body, right
on the ribs and, just before the bell put a
stop to the round, a left on Dixon's nead.
Dixon bored in In the seventh, landing
his left on the jaw and right on wind. ;
Curley blocked prettily, but landed only
onceâ a left on the body.
A straight lefi and a left hook sent j
Curley' a head back in the eighth. Curley |
was cautioned for "mugging" in a clinch, i
Near the end of the round Curley put '
straight lefts on the face and sent his
right on the jaw In a breakaway.
in the ninth Curley put his left to i
George's eye and landed a triple chopping !
blow on the chest, neck and head. Dixon !
rushed amj met with right on the body, ;
nd right on the face and right on i
wind. Curley was cautioned again for
"mugging."
Curley opened the tenth with a right to
the ribs and ;i left chop on the ear. Dixon j
put a hard Wt on the stomach, which i
made Curley wince. Dixon landed several
light body blows and Curley put a hard
right on the eye. Dixon failed to laini In
the eleventh until the bell rang and was!
evidently willing to let Curley tire himself j
by continuous leading. Curley landed a '
I right jolt on the body.
George opened the twelfth with a sting-
Ing left swing on the ear. Several light j
angea followed, and In close quarters!
George planted bis right on the body and '
hooked his left to the head, dropping
Curley to the floor. The Englishman
struggled to regain his feet, but the bell i
saved him after three seconds.
Curh-y came up groggy in the thirteenth
and Dixon fought to finish him. but Cur
ley improved fast. He put a left to Dix- j
fact and body and right over the '
i heart, and was in good condition at the !
I bell.
lii the fourteenth Curley put a straight
left to Dixon'B face and George put two
rights to the Jaw.
Cur!. . i . ! the fifteenth with a left
to face and Dixon jabbed left to Curley's
right eye, which was swollen from Cur
fall In the twelfth. Dlxon landed a
hard left on the stomach that seemed to
cause Curley great pain.
The exchanges in the sixteenth were
light. Curley struck Dixon from a clinch,
but immediately apologised.
The seventeenth, eighteenth and ntne
rounds were tame. Both men were
tired, Curley more so than Dlxon. Cur
ley made ;>n effort in the twentieth, get
, ting his left to the body, and though
Dlxon kept going to his man, his efforts
were Ineffective.
Dixon had a shade thp better of the
twenty-flnrt, but Curley put a left hook
to the jaw find woke Dixon up.
Curley began the twenty-second with a
rl^ht drive to Dixon's body. Dixon looked
, but hlfl expression did not scare
the ''.riton. who blocked and gnarded
;. H>- chopped a left to Dixon's
face and George sent a right to the body.
in the twenty-third Curley got his right
over to Dixon's jaw hard, but after this
effort his defense weakened. At the close
of the round Dixon landed a blow in the
wind that mnde Curley vomit as he went
to his corner.
tie's faking: look out for him.
George," s-aid O'Rourke as they came up
for the twenty-fourth. Dixon forced the
fighting, but was ineffective. Curley put
a left to the body at the close.
Jn the final round Dixon cut loose and
Bwunp for the heart. Curley met him with
a right on the body. Then they slusxert
without repanl to science. Curley went
to the ropes, but fought his way out
again, and at the bell they wre mixing
it' up in slam-bant? style. Referee W hite 3
decision In favor of Dixon met with ap
proval.

MORE MILLIONS FROM NOME
PORTLAND HERE WITH THREE
HUNDRED MINERS.
Charles W. Morgan Arrives From
Okhotsk Sea â Found Sixty-
Pounds of Anibergrii Dur
ing the Cruise.
Another vessel of the Alaska Commer
cial Company's fleet arrived from Cape
Nome, tit. Michael and Unalaska yester
day. Shortly alter daybreak the Portland,
wilh 304 returning miners, put in an ap
pearance and waa ducked at Fremont
street wharf. There was aboard ten
boxes of gold dust, each valued at about
Jlixi, UtKJ, for the company, and each miner
had all the way from $200 to $StWO worth
of dust in his possession.
Among those who came down on the
Portland was J. A. Dexter, who has been
on the Yukon and at Cape Nome since
1890. lie has done remarkably well and Is
considered one ol the wealthiest men that
has left the Â«oiu lields this year. Some
time ago he injured his knee, and li is
for a surgical operation thai he has come
to San Francisco. Â±Ie is accompanied by
his wife, who Is an Indian woman, and
his two ehliaren.
Louis siloss Jr. also returned on the
Portland. He has been on the- different
gold iieids for several months, looking
after the vast Interests of toe *. ummercial
Company, tie is in good health, but is
very much thinner man when he left
San Francisco.
The crew ot the Portland confirm the
stories ot typhoid fever, and some of the
passengers " were sick with vne disease
when they left Cape Nome. James K.
Girling, wnose death was reported by the
RoanoKe, passed away on the Portland in
Unalaska Harbor, lie was taken aboard
suffering from typhoid. There were ten
other fever patients on the Portland when
she left Nome, but five of them recovered
sufficiently during the passage to be able
to leave the ship when she docked. The
others were removed to the various hos
pitals during the day. The steamer was
examined by the quarantine officer, but
after a searching investigation into the
cases he allowed her to go to her wharf.
Captain Lundquist reports that the
steamers Cleveland. Alona and Albion,
the schooner Vega and bark Alaska were
at Cape Nome on October 18, while the
steamer Homer was off Cape Nome,
bound for St. Michael, on October 10.
There were at Dutch Harbor on October
23 the schooner Thomas F. Bayard and
; the steam whaler Kariuk. The latter was
to sail for San Francisco on October 24.
The catch of the whalers Is reported as
follows: Alexander 7. Balena V, Alaska

The whaling bark Charles W. .Morgan
arrived from the Okhotsk Sea during the
afternoon. She brought into port
barrels of sperm oil, 200 barrels of whale
: oil and 2600 pounds of whalebone. On
mber 3 the Morgan spoke the whal
ing bark California, with %*) barrels of
sperm and 400 barrels of whale oil and
SUOO pounds of whalebone.
It was one of the Charles W. Morgan's
boat crews that picked up sixty pounds of
ambergris, valued at Jjm.Ow, in the Ok
' hotsk Sea.. The boat was chasing a whale
when the valuable fine! was made. Since
i then the ambergris has been sold .;.>d the

ih.' vessel. The story of the nnd w<l.~, iuid

The Morgan lost one of her boats dur
-1 ing the cruise. Tin- Becond day out of the
1 Okhotsk Sea a whale was sighted and two
boats were lowered to give chase. First
Mate Morgan was in command of one and
: Fourth Mate Thurston of the other.
j Thurston harpooned the leviathan and a
I few minutes later Morgan put another
steel barb into it. The whale became in-
I furiated, and making for Morgan's boat
! smashed it like an egg shell. The men
i saw the monster coming and saved them
selves by jumping overboard.
In order to save his shipmates Thurston
I cut loose from the whale and it escaped i
I with two of the Morgan's harpoons sttck
! Ing in its body. The men were picked
1 up and put aboard the whaler and the
Morgan proceeded on her way to San

The schooner Maggie C. Rups is coming
i here for repairs. While passing out of

street, Company C of the League of the
Cross Cadet? will give a very attractive
entertainment in aid of its uniform fund.
A musical programme of a high order
will be presented by the best local pro
fessional tali lit. Captain Dennis Geary,
late of the California Heavy Artillery,
United States volunteers, will' deliver an
nddress on "The American Soldier in the
Philippines," and It is reported that he
will reveal startling facts concerning the
desecration of churches and church prop
erty In the Philippines.
It Is also expected that Rev. P. C.
Yorke, the late chancellor of the arch
diocese, will Ie present, and will act as
a vice president He Is expected to ar
rive from the Bast, via Los Angeles, early
in the evening. When he w;is spiritual
director of th.- League of the Cross and
chaplain of the cadet regiment, C Com
pany was his favorite.

WELCOME TO THE
GALLANT KANSANS
Topeka Streets Crowded
With Visitors.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TOPEKA. Kan., Nov. 2.â Returning
warriors never received a more fitting
welcome home than that accorded to-day
to the soldiers of "Kansas' Famous Fight-
Ing Twentieth" on its arrival from San
Francisco. General Frederick Funston
came in for the lion's share of attention
shown the officers, and the "little hero of
Manila" was literally taken oft his feet
by his admirers In their eagerness to dis
play their regard for him.
At every station in Kansas, after the
Colorado line was crossed early this
morning, cheering crowds met the sol
diers and greeted them in unstinted fash
ion. Schools and colleges along the line
were closed, business was practically sus
pended and the people nocked to the
trains by the thousands.
Snow in the mountains had delayed the
trains and it was 12:55 this afternoon be
fore the first -section reached Topeka.
The three other sections followed closely,
the last squad of soldiers being landed
shortly before 4 o'clock.
So many thousands of people were on
the platform and the railroad tracks that
the train had to fairly crawl into the de
pot. Whistles screeched, cannon boomed,
dozens of bands played and the cheers
were almost deafening.
From every car window leaned soldiers,
waving hats and yelling and whooping
shrill hoorays. They leaped from the car
steps before the train stopped, and fell
into the arms of friends, who grasped and
hugged them and passed them on to eager
relatives and sweethearts.
Fathers and mothers pushed through
the crowd in a frantic endeavor to find
their boys." Many affecting scenes were
enacted.
General and Mrs. Funston were In the
rear Pullman of the first section. When
the train came to a stop there was a rush
to g't to them, and It took half a dozen
strong men to keep the people off the car
platform. A woman spied Mrs. Funston
at a win low and pushed her baby up to be
kissed by the generals wife. Mrs. Fun-
Bton complied promptly, arrd immediately
dozens of mothers reached forward with
their babies to be kissed.
Funston was hardly off the cars before
he was half-dragged, half-carried, up
stairs into the dining room.
Solid blocks of people tilled every street
leading to the depot, and the city was
taxed to its utmost to care for its guests.
It Beemed as if the whole State of Kansas
had come to town, and It Is estimated
that fully 60,000 outsiders were here. Tons
of bunting had been used in decorating
the buildings, and electrical and other
decorations were used with a lavish hand.
With the exasperating and unavoidable
break in the arrival of the trains bear
ing the .-oldier?, it was after 4 o'clock
when the parade began.
A guard of honor for the boys of the
Twentieth wns the Civil War veteran's,
the members of the National Guard and
many civic organizations. There were
thirty-two bands in the parade, the per
formers numbering nearly 1000 musicians.
The entire parade line, over two miles,
was a mass of cheering humanity.
General Funston with his wife rode in
the carriage of honor, while Colonel Met
caif. on a white charger, and Lieutenant
General Little, on a prancing black, took
their appointed stations at the head of
the regiment. All received ovations at
every point of the line.
At" the State House the military orders
were drawn up in line at the south steps.
chief Justice Doster in a brief Bpeech
introduced Genera] J. K. Hudson, who, on
behalf of the people of Kansas, presented
the beautiful $1000 sword to General
Puns ton.
In a short speech of acceptance General
Funston paid he predicted that in one
year's time the island of Luzon would
be as quiet as Massachusetts. "I return
to Manila," said he, "and unless I get
sick or Usabled, I will not return until
the war Is over. "
Colonel Metcalf followed General
Funston in a brief speech. Later in the
evening an elaborate banquet was served
in the new State library rooms to the
soldiers and officers of the Twentieth.
! The day Â« losed with the most magnificent
! display" of fireworks In the evening ever
| given in the State's history.

Debs in Los Angeles.
LOS ANGELES, Nov. 2.â Eugene V.
Dobs, the labor leader, arrived in the city
this morning from San Francisco. It had
been supposed that Mr. Debs would not
arrive until to-morrow and consequently
there was no reception for him. lie is at
an uptown hotel and evidently trying to
keep in retirement. He speaks here to
morrow night.

MUST ALL PROVE
THEIR RIGHT TO
RETAIN COMMAND
Six Volunteer Officers
in Trouble.
ORDERED FOR EXAMINATION
THREE RESIGNED WHEN THE
OBDEB WAS ISSUED.
Weeding Out Men Who Have liearned
Nothing From One "War and
Wish to Fight in An
other.
Those officers of the Forty-sixth Regi
ment who have not taken the pains to
qualify themselves for the positions they
hold are having a hard time. Six of them
have been ordered before a board of reg
ular cfflcers to be examined as to their
ntness to perform the duties of their
rank. Three of the officers resigned as
soon as the orders for the board were
issued, but the other three are going to
take the risk.
The six officers of the Forty-sixth or
dered up for examination are: Captain
William B. Thomas, Captain Lewis Pate
bone, First Lieutenant Wallace W. Batch
elder, First Lieutenant Benjamin P. Luk
ens, Second Lieutenant George W. Earl
and Second Lieutenant Carlos W. Pierce.
Batchelder, Lukens and Pierce resigned,
and Thomas was up before the board yes
terday His examination was not con
cluded! but it will be this morning, and
then the board will take up the others.
The board is composed of Lolonei Â±i % ts.
Freeman, Twenty-fourth Infantry; Co -
onel Jacob B. Rawles, Third Artillery;
Captain Sedgwick Pratt, Third Artillerj,
ana Second Lieutenant William i orse,
Third Artillery, recorder.
In each case there is some specific
charge of incompetence or neglect of duty
preferred by the colonel of the regiment,
and this particular charge, as well as
their general ntness, will be gone into by
the board. Â
In the formation of all the second call
regiments the colonels were notified that
the officers would be assigned to the regi
ments without examination, the only
qualification being that they had served
in the war with Spain. If, however, at
the end of a certain time any officer
showed that he was incapable of perform
ing the duties he had undertaken the
colonel was directed to apply for a board
..I examination and have the officer or
dered to appear before it. This is what
the colonel of the Forty-sixth is doing.
The report of the board will be sub
mitted to department headquarters and
from there it will be sent to Washington.
The â officers examined will probably re
sign if they believe the report of the
board will go against thorn; but at any
rate, if it he not favorable, General Shaf
ter will not allow them to proceed to
Manila before army headquarters has
been heard from.
It is the intention of the department to
establish a permanent, hospital at the
Presidio in place of the general hospital
now there. This does not necessarily
mean that the buildings will be changed,
but they will be fitted up with all the
modern appliances to make them more
comfortable. It was not intended in the
ftrsi place to establish a permanent gen
eral hospital at the Presidio, but that is
now the intention.
Private George Burr. Company C of the
Forty-nfth Infantry, was seriously
wounded Wednesday night by the acci
dental discharge of his rifle. It was loaded
and It- was cleaning it. and the cartridge
was exploded, the bullet passing through
his left arm at the elbow. The arm was
amputated at the general hospital yes
terday.
HIS BRILLIANT RECORD.
Alfred P. Black Has Proved Himself
to Be the Right Man for Dis
trict Attorney.
Alfred P. Black is remarkably well
equipped to serve the people of San Fran
cisco as District Attorney. He has had
â  wide experience, having been identified
' with the District Attorney's office for a
I long time, and at present he holds the
position of Chief Deputy District Attor
ney. Mr. Black has conducted at least
1000 criminal trials in the city of San
â¢ Francisco and has been remarkably sue
; cessful. During the present year he has
I rendered characteristic service's. Out of
eighty-six important cases committed to
! his charge he has secured sixty-six con
victions, which Is a noteworthy record.
Mr. Black is possessed >>f a rare judicial
â  quality which is so important in the men
tal make-up of a public prosecutor.
While he does not wish to see a guilty
man escape, yet if there is the slightest
doubt in his mind with regard to the cul
pability of the prisoner he is inclined to
lean to mercy's side and will not insist
on the extreme penalty, except in cases
which seem to demand it. If he thinks
the defendant Innocent he win say so and
end the prosecution. Alfred P. Black de
serves and will receive the support of the
good people of San Francisco for the of-
Bee of District Attorney. His splendid
record in the past is a guaranty for the
future.
In the Divorce Court.
Emma M. Brown was granted a divorce
from James A. Brown on the ground of
extreme cruelty. Decrees of divorce have
also been granted to Sylvia Neuman from
Walter S. Neuman on the ground of de
sertion and Franz A. Uenkel from Hen
rietta Henkel on the ground of desertion.
Bertha Tanzman has sued Abraham
Tanzman for a divorce, alleging cruelty
hs a cause of action. Suits for divorce
on the ground of desertion have been
filed by J- W. Wardale against Jessica
Wardale and Belle Gans against Robert
Gans.

WACHENDORF IS FREE.
Judge Cook Discharges Him From
Custody and Dismisses the
Jury.
Dr. Christian C. J. Wachendorf, who
has been on trial for the murder of John
Kchlenbeck, was ordered discharged from
custody and the jury dismissed by Judge
Cook yesterday. Some of the stars of the
detective department reached the conclu
sion that Kehlenbeck came to his death
through the administration of morphine
by his roommate. Dr. Wachendorf. They
based their conclusion on the facts that
Kehlenbeck was dead, and that as he had
a few hundred dollars it was sufficient
motive for Wachendorf to murder him In
hopes of getting the gold.
Expert witnesses, however, were una
ble to ascertain the cause of Kehlen
beck's death. District Attorney Solomons
passed several days in an endeavor to
prove that Kehlenbeck had died other
than a natural death, but *falled. Judge
Cook then toÂ«k tbe matter in his own

Came to Help Horace Davis.
Mrs. Kate M. Flynn. assistant secretary
of the State Board of Examiners at Sac
ramento, obtained a week's leave of ab
sence ft>r the purpose of visiting her homa
In this city to aid in the election of Hon.
Horace Davis.
Mrs. Flynn. while not a politician in tha
crude sense of tno word, which brings la
dies-of delicate sensibilities in contact
with the professional politician, Is never
theless a lady with a host of personal
friends who will give ear to her solicita
tions when asking their votes for Mr
Davis.
Being a native daughter and also a re
tired school teacher of the San Francisco
public schools, she wields an influence
among the enlightened people. She Is foi
Davis and the entire Republican ticket
