DEMOCRATS ON PHELAN.
THERE is something direct and potent in the
expression of their opinions by the straight
Democrats who support Dr. Cleveland for
Mayor and reject Phelatt. At their meeting in Met
ropolitan Hal! a spade was not called a teaspoon
nor a pickax a toothpick. Their principal speaker.
Mr Harris concluded his arraignment of Pheian by
asking his hearers to "save this city from bossism
infinitely more absolute, vicious and intolerant than
, any which has ever reared its hydra head in San
Francisco. Do this by voting against the
Phelanistic-Examineristn, which is an unequivocal
ety, dangerous to public florals, an in
to the public conscience, to clean journalism,
clean government and genuine progress, and is op
posed to commercial and industrial enterprise, to
true Democracy and in general to that fair fame of
San Francisco, which all good citizens desire to up- j
hold." .
In quoting this we cannot say the ' italics are
--." for in accent and emphasis it is all the work
recent political associate and supporter of Mr.
Pheian. If it cast a light on the situation which
makes men blink, it is not from Republican but
Democratic sources.
These gentlemen have grown familiar with the
Mayor's methods by intimate association with him
in the same party, and they are supposed to testify
from abtindance of knowledge. They have the nat
ural feeling of party men who desire that party vic
tories shall be properly credited, and the proposition
to have a personal victory for Mr. Pheian, ignoring
the party, is repugnant to them.
From their frequent and frank expressions it is evi
dent that they not only resent his assumption of
being better than his party, if not. too good for it
altogether, but they regard him as not as good as
the party, and put forward his methods as evidence
â¢' it
Nothing done by a common, unshaven boss was
ever grosser than Phelan's forcing of Geary's nomi
nation and his remark that "Geary may be a scrub.
,bi:t we must have a soldier on the ticket."
In ridiculous background to this is Geary's declara
tion that Pheian is "our war Mayor" and his intima
tion that the war Mayor's influence reached the Phil
ippines, and was more potent than the Federal Gov
ernment in nerving our soldiers to pursue the Fili
pino skedaddlers. No wonder there is some nau*ea
in the pit of the old Democratic stomach at the
spectacle of a war Mayor and a scrub standing as
candidates of the old party while denying its existence
and ignoring its principles. Dr. Cleveland, Mr. Tlar
-lis and their colleagues are certainly entitled to pub
lic respect for their attitude on straight politics.
The Pheian leadership by Gavin McNab suggests
other funny combinations and strange bedfellowships.
When McNab controlled the committee for the cele
bration of the Fourth of July he was so rancorous
against union labor organizations that he prevented
the employment of union bands to play for the pro
>ii. The result was that no labor organizations
appeared in the parade, and Barry's Star opened
every vial of its celebrated wrath on him and changed
his name in its columns to "Gavin McScab." Now
McNab is pulling the stroke oar for Pheian. and
the Star i* in noisy accord with the combination.
Such a mixture of fish, flesh and fowl makes the
gorge rise in the throat of old-fashioned Democ
racy, and its representatives are warranted in oppos
ing such a political burgoo.
One of the surprises of the Ohio campai^^ has
been the success achieved by Senator Ilanna as n
stump orator. It was known that he is a good or
ganizer, but doubts were felt concerning his ability
to make a speech and mix with the people. These
have now been dissipated and it appears there arc
no mass-meetings of the canvass more largely at
tended than those at which he is announced as to
speak.
The announcement of further difficulties in the way
of the settlement of the Samoan question is a matter
of no surprise. When three great nations like the
United States, Great Britain and Germany try to get
on to so small a group of islands with both feet there
is bound to be trouble.
A NOTABLE PETITION.
NF.YF.R before did there go from the people of
rnia so strong a petition for the appoint
ment of a particular individual to a political
office as that which will request President McKinley
to appoint William R. Wheeler to the vacancy in the
Interstate Commerce Commission. That much can
he safely said, for among the organizations signing
the petition through their officials are some which,
like the Merchants' Association of this city and the
Chamber of Commerce of Los Angeles, have never
before urged upon an appointing power the selection
of a particular candidate for any office.
The extent to which all the commercial and in
dustrial organizations of the entire Pacific Coast have
united in supporting the candidacy of Mr. Wheeler
is not only complimentary to him but is significant
of the prevailing feeling of the people of the coast
States upon the issue at stake. Our merchants and
manufacturers have now to meet the aggressive
claims and demands of Chicago and St. Louis and at
the same time guard against the schemes of the
Southern Pacific Company. Just why the Southern
Pacific should be at this time antagonizing
the coast is not clear, hut the antagonism is mani
fest, and in defending our interests before the Inter
state Commerce Commission as -.veil as in seeking
to obtain the appointment of a Pacific Coast repre
sentative on the commission, the merchants and
manufacturers have to beware of railroad hostility at
home as well as the open contest made by the big
cities of the Mississippi Valley.
The petition to the President sets forth that the
delay in it-; presentation has been due solely to the
determination of the signers not to indorse any one
for the place until such time as they could indicate
to the President "the unanimous choice of the busi
ness interests of the whole coast." They are now
able to do that. As the petition says: "A conven
tion has just been held in this city of the Pacific
Const Jobbers' and Manufacturers' Association,
which was attended by representatives from every
line of business from the States of Washington, Ore
gon and California. At that convention the matter
of indorsing some one for the vacancy caused by the
resignation of Mr. Calhoun was taken up, and Mr.
W. R. Whfe'er of this city was the choice, without
c-nc dissenting vote."
The organizations which unite in the petition in
clude almost every important commercial or indus
trial association on the coast. Mr. Wheeler therefore
st.'.nds as the representative of the business interests
of this section of the Union, and those who oppose
his candidacy are virtually opposing the selection of
any man from the coast at all. Fortunately no op
position has thus far manifested itself except that of
the Southern Pacific and the organs of that com
pany. Even that opposition ought now to cease. It
TIIE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, NOVKMItEK 8, 1899,
has been declared over and over again that in the
movement for protecting the trade interests of the
coast there is designed no injury to the railroad. It
will, in fact, serve the welfare of the roads to build
up the trade of California, Oregon and Washington.
Mr. Wheeler represents a force which is working
for the benefit of all, and his indorsement by the
people of the coast should be made unanimous.
Congressman Roberts, the polygarnist, against
whom the shafts of scores of societies are aimed, has
at last found a champion. She is a woman, lives in
Chicago, and insists, with a woman's logic, that
Roberts ought to be seated as a member of the House
of Representatives. Her rftasoa is the customary
ladylike one of "simply because."
IU R. E. L. COLNOX of the Stockton Mail
/ \ goes to the trouble and expense of lampoon
ing the proprietor and manager of The Call.
He indulges in most extraordinary statements of his
code of ethics and exudes malice from his intellectual
pores until the result makes him appear like a hip
popotamus indulging iji a red sweat. In defense of
his code of morals Mr. Colnon says: "What would
be grossly immoral in one country might be quite the
proper thing in another." And to this he adds: "A
newspaper is not an agency for the elevation of the
morals of a people and cannot be made such."
We are sure that all readers of the Stockton Mail
acquit it of any attempt to elevate morals, or even
to let them stay as good as it found them.
Because The Call has said that certain leprous
practices can be limited by letting the light of pub
licity fall upon them Mr. Colnon is sure that we in
tend to blackmail somebody. We don't know what
he has been doing lately that he ducks his head when
ever expo-nre is mentioned.
His reference to the proprietor of The Call in con
nection with the subject of blackmail sug-gests the
attempt he made to stand up the Valley Railroad for
a large sum of money and its exposure to the direct
erf, by the proprietor of The Call. Mr. Colnon has
certainly lived down to his code of ethics, not only
in journalism but in office. While people were com
pelled to do public business with him as secretary to
the Governor of California they were not long in
finding out that he held peculiar views as to the op
portunities of his position. When application was
sent through him for removal of penal disabilities,
with full explanation of the case, he did not con
sider the reasons sufficient for consideration and re
quested the parties concerned to "dig up" sufficient
testimony in the case to justify his attention to it.
How many may have "'dug up" and who, during his
term as Secretary, are things unknown to the pub
lic. But as he considers what is immoral in one
country may be highly moral in another, the transfer
of that idea to official life is easy and he would find
no difficulty in making it. As he stood at one end
of the process of "digging up" and the digger stood
at the other, what to the digger seemed highly im
moral, to Mr. Colnon may have seemed moral and
proper, as he insists that right and wrong are not
actual, but are interchangeable according to the
point of view.
When the "digging up" probably Rot bad, Colnon
\va=i transferred to the Harbor Commission and be
came its president. His record there i< written in
the judicial findings in the case of Irwin vs. the
commission. From the testimony in that case it is
evident that the water front was in luck that it did
not get carried off. It was said that even the clock
in the ferry tower, having its good manners cor
rupted by evil associations, was frequently seen to
hold one hand behind its back, and the time ball
bn athed freer every day when it slid up the pole pre
paratory to it? meridian fall, for things that could
rcost high were safer.
We have no doubt that Mr. Colnon's secret soul
utters itself out of hi; personal experience when he
- in his lampoon: "Publicity of crime increases
crime. The Call's fool notion that the fear of pub
licity will deter those wrongly inclined is all that
could be expected from a man with half a head."
We admit that from the standpoint of our ex
perience with Colnon he i-- right. We have no rea
son to believe that any fear of publicity, or even the
glare of a judicial exposure, ever made him bat an
eye or drove him off the trail of a job that he had in
sight or scent.
Our only justification is that we believed him to be
the exception that proves the rule. We do not pre
tend that any publicity would affect him. but we
hope there are others, whose good name has not yet
become a reminiscence, who would turn away from
the lure of passion or greed and put temptation be
hind them for sheer pride in a good reputation which
exposure and publicity would destroy.
In this world, where traps arc set on every hand
and where the steadfastness of men is often shaken
by the example of flourishing iniquity to which Mr.
Colnon has been a contributor, society requires that
no influence should be omitted that will keep men
in the straight path. If the fear of publicity is one
such influence, the newspaper which holds it over the
man that is poising between right and wrong, uncer
tain whether to take the plunge or stand upright, is
a proper agency in the elevation of morals which
prevents transgression by the certainty of exposure.
While publicity did not succeed in reforming Col
non, the fear of it may so deter others as to leave
him the distinction of standing alone, a monument
of incorrigibility, standing on his reputation like a
crow on a carcass, cawing his belief that all meat is
carrion.
A late dispatch from Ladysmith announcing that
a number of stragglers from the regiments captured
by the Boers have made their escape and returned
to that city, adds: "A number of mules with a por
tion of the mountain battery have also come in." It
is therefore evident the mule has not been so pesky
as was reported. By bringing back some of the gins
he has done something at [east to atone for stam
peding and upsetting the ammunition wagon.
When the United States acquired the Sulu Islands
there was a distinct understanding that the re
ligious beliefs of the natives were to be respected.
Among the beliefs thus honored is one which makes
the killing of Christians a passport to paradise. This
very interesting fact may suggest, perhaps, that the
Sulu Islands are first-class places for enthusiastic
missionaries to shun.
The Republican party lms put a clean ticket in the
municipal contest, and if the voters give it a clean
victory they will receive in return a clean adminis
tration in every branch of the city government.
According to the rulings of Justice Rodden of San
Rafael the game laws are dead in Mario County.
How niceâ dead game laws for dead game sports.
A railroad is to be constructed in Persia under Rus
sian supervision on money furnished by French cap
italists. Now who will own the road?
ABOUT BLACKMAIL.
THE COLOSSAL STATUE
DESIGNED BY A QUEEN
R<~>ME, Sept. 10.â The colossal statue of the Virgin, designed by Queen Mar
gherita herself, has been unveiled and dedicated. This enormous bronze
rests on a massive pedestal on inaccessible Rocca Melone, one of the hlgh
fst peaks in the Italian Alps. The statue was paid for by the contributions of
150.000 school children, their tribute to the Virgin Mother in grateful commemora
tion of the rescue of the members of a company of Alpine militia from an aval
ancheâa rescue so remarkable that the soldiers ascribed it to the direct influence
of the Virgin, to whom they prayed in their strep?.
Graven in the pedestal is an invocation from the Pope praying the Virgin to
guard the frontier of Italy.

SARAH COMSTOCK.
NEW COURT RULE.
! Supreme Court Changes the Order for j
Holding Examinations.
At every quarterly examination by the
Supreme Court of applicants for admis
sion to practice the number of would-be -
lawyers is astonishing , and the number !
seems to be increasing. The Justices have !
j decided to change the mode of holding ex- ,
' aminations, for yesterday they issued the
following new order:
Order Amending Order Relating to Examina
tions of Applicants for Admission to Practice |
| Law â Applicants for admission to practice law
| will not hereafter be examined in open court
I at the sessions held in Sacramento and Pan !
i Francisco. In pursuance of the amendment :
; to section 27C of the Code of Civil Procedure |
the Commissioners of the Supreme Court or!
any three of them are hereby designated to |
j conduct public examinations of all persons who
present applications filed in due form. Such
; examinations will be held at the Supreme Court
rooms In Ban Francisco on the second Monday :
' in March, June. September and December of
. each year. No person rejected shall be at
I liberty to renew the application earlier than \
' the second regular examination next after such :
â  rejection. ! Until otherwise ordered applicants
; who are bona fide residents of any of the
' counties of the I,os Angeles district will, if i
; they so desire, be examined at the beginning
i of each session of the court In Los Angeles
!as heretofore. The next examination will be :
j held December 11, 1599.

WHlTEWASHâ Subscriber, City. The
following' receipt for whitewash is the one
sent out by the Lighthouse Board of the
United States Treasury Department. It
has been found by experience, to answer
on wood, brick and stone nearly aa well
as oil paint and is much cheaper: "Slack
one-half bushel of unslacked lime witn
hoilintr water, keeping it covered during
the process. Strain it and add a peck Oi
salt, dissolved In warm water; three
pounds of ground rice put in boiling wa
ter and boiled to a thin paste; half a
pound of powdered Spanish wniting and a
pound of clear glue, dissolved in warm
water; mix these well together, and let
the mixture stand for several days. Keep
the wash thus prepared in a kettle or
portable furnace, and when used put it on
as hot as possible with a painter s or a
whitewash brush."
SOUTH SEA ISLANDSâ M.. Placerville,
Cal. Any first-class bookseller can fur
nish literature on the South Sea Islands
and books on boatbuilding. The question
"Are the South Sea Islands a good place
for a young man to go for business? is
too broad to admit of a general answer.
You should select the island you would
like to go to, and if there is a I nited
States Consul thf-re. write to him, tell ng
him what line of business you would like
to engage in, and he will advise you.
MAN-OF-WARâ S., City. Man-of-war
is a phrase applied to a line-of-battle ship,
contrary to the usual rule of the English
language by which all ships are feminine.
Talbot in "Words and Their Origin"
"The use of this term probably ar
the following manner: Men-of-war were
heavily armed soldiers. A ship full of
them would be called ;i man-of-war ship.
In process of time the word ship was dis
carded as unnecessary, and there re
mained the phrase man-of-war." After
the "ship"' was dropped from the phrase
man-of-war became the appellation of the
vessel instead of the man. as originally,
and now the individual is known as a
man-of-war's man. The plural of man-nf
war. either individual or vessel, is men-of
war.

lan from defeat.

out Democrat with ordinary perceptive
i faculties ought to understand the drift of

j will leave no resource untouched to win
j out. If it becomes necessary to knife men

of conscience. -When a Democrat as high-

It is perhaps unfortunate for the honest

the ticket. It must not be supposed that

BUSINESS MEN TURN
OUT TO HEAR DAVIS
issues of \\i% Campaign Made
Cleir at Pioneer Hail.
The issues of the present campaign were
never more clearly presented than at the
meeting held last night at ~ ioneer Hall,
nndt-r the auspices of the Horace Davis
Business Men's Club. S. H. Kent presided
and the hail and gallery were filled and
standing n >m was a 1 a premium long be
fore the lirsr speaker was announced.
\Viil San Francisco, which has profited
SO largely from the policy of the Repub
lican administration, slap that administra
tion in the face and say that It disap
proves that policy by electing the Phelan
ticket? That was the question put by
every speaker to every man in the audi
ence. That was the question each speak
er answered with a rousing "no," and the
applause that greeted the answer was
proof that it was the heartfelt sentiment
of the meeting.
"No!" thundered the speaker. "San
Francisco will not be so foolish as to do
anything of the kind. She will elect the
ticket beaded by Horace Davis and flash
the answer back to McKlnley that the
people of Ban Francisco, Idle when he
went Into office, approve of his policy that
has set them at work in the van of pros
perity."
Chairman Kent was in the midst of his
opening address, when Horace Davis en
tered the hall and ;h>- meeting broke otit
Into applause, to which tin- candidate for
Mayor bowed right and left as he walked
to the platform, where he was given three
cheers more. He was introduced almost
Immediately afterward, and began an ad
dress which showed that the campaign
was not wearing on his powers. He began,
addressing his hearers as business men,
by talking business. What the city need
ed, he said, was a business administra
tion.
"If I am elected Mayor," he said. "I
would first attend to what I deem to be
the essential requirements of the city, and
my first desire would be that the city
should have good Bewers, good hospitals
aiid good schools. The city government
is a great corporation and requires to l><
managed with the same prudence and
economy we practice In our own affairs.
1 do not believe it is good policy to run
the corporation Into debt. I do not believe
It Is good economy to spend money unless
you know what you are spending it forâ
unless you can pet quid pro quo."
After eulogizing his colleagues on the
ticket Mr. Davis had something to say of
the administration's anxiety concerning
the San Francisco election.
"Now. my friends." he said, "the pros
perity of Ran Francisco is the result of
the wise policy of the Republican admin
istration. 4t is the wise protective policy
In the case of the manufacturer and the
expansive policy in the ease of the com
mercial interestsâ and I want to say to
you that the administration is asking you
to hold up its hands. It is looking with
anxiety to the result of elections in dif
ferent States and this flection far away,
the only sign to be seen on this coast
as to whether the people are in sympathy
with the policy of the administration. It
is looking: with anxiety to see whether
you are holding up its hands or whether
you are turning your backs upon this
prosperity and turn down the adminis
tration.
"Now, my friends, It Is not a matter of
indifference if it is telegraphed across the
continent on November 8 that San Fran
cisco has gone Democraticâ that the city
that stood nearest to our new possessions
and profited most from its policy had
turned down the administration. I can
never believe that this city will be guilty
of such ingratitude and such lack of com
mercial intelligence as to put the Demo
crats in power on the 7th of November."
After three rousing cheers for Mr. Davis
D. E. MeKinlay was introduced to the
audience. He devoted himself, when not
interrupted by applause, to pointing out
the uses to which Mr. Phelan and Mr.
Davis had put their wealth. In the rec
ord of the present Mayor he found that
no industry had been started to help
labor. In that of Mr. Davis he found all
the capital Invested in enterprises em
ploying labor.
Attorney General Tlrey I^. Ford made a
telling argument in favor of the return of
the Republican candidates.
"The verdict of San Francisco on Tues
day next," he said, "will be heralded
throughout tills country either as an In
dication that San Francisco favors the
expansion of her commerce or that she
would join with Mr. Atkinson and Mr.
Bryan and others who would withdraw
from the Philippines and. cowardly, trail
back to our own home again. I do not
believe that the business men of San
Francisco are going to permit it. I do
not believe that the wage-earners are go
ing to permit it. I do not believe they
are going to turn hack the hands of pro"
grosy on the dial of events."

MISSION DISTRICT
SOLID FOR DAVIS
Thirty=Fiftli District Republi
cans Hold a Housing Rally.
The Republicans of the Thirty-fifth As
sembly District gave evidence last night
that the Mission Is going to roll up a
rousing big vote f<ir Horace Davis. The
rally held at Twenty-lifth and Valencia
streets was one of the largest, most
earnest and most enthusiastic political
gatherings ever hold in that section of the
City. I-tmg before the .speakers appeared
it was evident that the hall would not be
large enough to accommodate the citizens
who were anxious and proud to show
their fealty to the Republican cadidates
and Republican principles. A large
crowd, unable to find room inside, or
ganized an overflow meeting on the side
walk. When those inside applauded the
outsiders took the rue and made the Mis
sion ring with their cheers and shouts for
DaViS and good municipal government.
Tiie reception given Mr. Davis took on
the air ot an ovation.
W. Warren, president of the Thirty
fifth Assembly District Republican Club,
under whose auspices the rally was given,
presided. Without any preliminaries he
Introduced as the first speaker of the even
ing Lucius Solomons, who placed Horace
Davis in nomination before the Republi
can Municipal Convention. He Impressed
upon the audience the fact that the ap-
H< aching election Would be the most im
portant e\er held in the city, and dem
onstrated by an able argument that the
election Of Horace Davis was necessary
to show that the people of the West ap
prove the administration of President Mc-
Kinlfey and the principles of the Republi
can party. Mr. Solomons asserted that the
reason Mr. Phelan fought shy of national
politics was because lie had none. No
one, according to the speaker, had been
able to tell whether he was a gold or a
free-silver Democrat, ;; tree trader or a
protect irfnist, an expansionist or an antl
exjiansionist.
John Murphy, who was in the employ of
Horace Davis for eight years, said that
he felt it was a duty of gratitude he
owed the Republican candidate to rec
ommend him to his fellow workingmen.
"I am working for t'ncle Sam now,"
said Mr. Murphy. "You know he is a
pretty good boss, but from my experience
I know he is not a bit better than Horace
Davis. " He said that the Republican can
didate has always stood up to elevate
labor.
Colonel T. V. Eddy followed and made
an address that aroused all the enthu
siasm and fire in the audience. By way
i of Introductory he said that he never felt
' smaller in hts life. He had, by mistake,
found his way into a nearly Democratic
meeting and was preparing to make an
address when he discovered His error. He
said the issue of the campaign was not
home rule, as claimed by the Phelanites,
but as to who should rule.
HORACE DAVIS THE
CHAMPION OF LABOR
If the workingmen of San Francisco
have not forgotten the great work done
in behalf of organized white labor by
Horace Davis in years gone by his elec
tion as Mayor of this city is certain, for
they will vote for him almost to a man.
It is nearly twenty years since Horace
Davis served in Congress, and the record
he made while representing the people of
this section of the State in the national
capital lor four years made him the idol
of the white laborers of the coast.
That was when California was con
fronted with the problem at protecting
the labor of its wnite citizens from the
disastrous competition of swarms of Chl
i;. .-â¢â  coolies who were pouring into this
country from the Orient. In the great
battle for the restriction of Chinese labor
Horace Davis loomed up as the champion
of the laboring man, and it was through
his diligent and aggressive work that the
passage of the Chinese exclusion act was
linally secured.
Horace Davis was at that time fully
appreciated by the workingmen, and no
man in all California was more popular
cr better loved by the men who earned
their bread by the sweat of their brows,
Practically every labor organization in
til'- city adopted resolutions indorsing
him and praising him for the good work
he had dune and which he continued to
do as long as he remained in Congress.
The workingmen of San Francisco or
ganised torchlight parades and marched
"through the streets of the city shouting
the praises of Horace Davis With wild,
enthusiasm. In countless other .ways they
gave expression to their gratitude and
appreciation. Horace Davis was their
idol. .
Even before he was sent to Congress
Horace Davis was universally recognized
as. the true friend of the workingman; a
man whom they could trust and whose
word was as good as his bond; a man in
the wisdom of whose friendly counsel
they placed absolute faith, a faith that
was never shaken.
It is possible that time may have dulled
the memory of some . of those who knew
Horace Davis in those- days, but when
these incidents are recalled, to them they
will not fail to recollect that he was their
benefactor and their friend at all times
and under all circumstances.
The Horace Davis of to-day is the same
Horace Davis of twenty years ago. The
Intervening years have but added to his

111. JOHN RANDOLPH.
(John Randolph of Roanoke was for
many years the most striking figure in
American political life. So great were
his vagaries that many believed he was
not wholly sane. He was born In 1773 and
died in 1533. In early life he gave signs
of his unbalanced nature, for before he
was five years old he would give way to
ungovernable fits of anger, swooning
from the violence of his passion. As a
boy, in a Southern slave-holding; com
munity, he grew up with little restraint
upon his naturally Imperious will. In
public life he placed little if any check
upon his temper, which manifested Itself
in Invectives of wonderful violence and
insolence, showing an utter disregard of
the feelings of those who were the vic
tims of his tongue lashings. Probably no
man in American public life has ever sur
passed him In rancorous abuse of politi
cal opponents. When a member of the
House of Representatives he would enter
the hall, tall and gaunt of figure, booted
and spurred, whi ;.'â â â â â â â â â â â â â â 
in hand, and takcH
part at once ml
whatever b v sincssH
was going on, show-H
ing plainly his con-H
tempt for anythingM
or any person notH
suiting his fancy.M
Indeed, he was thel
very Incarnation nt *l
the slave mastnrH
spirit, a true expo-M
nent of "plantationH
manners," whi chH
are now happilyH
only a memory. J^P^P^P^P^P^P^P^JH
He entered puhlicM
life in 1799, when heH
offered himself forM
election to CongressM
as the champion Â°'H
State s o vcrelgnty.M
The period was al
critical one. ThoH
young nation hadH
been insulted byH
France, and a wavcH
of patriotic indigna-H
tion had swept overH
the land, bringing t >H
the unpopular Fed-B
eralists a great "P-H
portunity to streng-HJ
then themselves liyl
a firm but modi-rat.
course. This oppor-B
tunity they had â â â â â â â â â 
foolishly thrown away by passing the
alien and sedition laws, which at once
brought into play the determined ' hos
tility of the Republicans. The latter had
from the first dreaded the establishment
of a national government strong enough
to control and perhaps subvert the State
governments, and they believed that
these laws were a long stride In that di
rection. To meet this bold move of the
Federalists, and at the same time bring
Into prominence the State as an essential
factor in our scheme of government, the
Republican Legislatures of Virginia and
Kentucky, under the lead of Madison and
Jefferson, issued the Virginia and Ken
tucky resolutions. These resolutions de
liberately ignored the Supreme Court,
and proposed that the constitutionality
of an obnoxious law should be submitted
for decision to the several States of the
Union. This evidently transferred the
final exercise of sovereignty from the na
tion to the States. Had it prevailed the
national edifice reared by the convention
in 1787, and strengthened with such care
by Hamilton and the Federalists in Con
gress, would have fallen to the ground.
Washington, seeing the danger, prevailed
upon Patrick Henry to come forward in
defense of the national authority, and
this he did. though Teak from sickness
and years, with an outburst of patriotic
ardor which brought to mind his ancient
fire. This speech was answered by Ran
dolph, who upheld the sovereignty of the
States as against the nation, and this
was the first important occasion on which
he displayed that eloquence which was
always po effective with a Southern audi
ence.
He -went to Congress and was several
times re-elected by his faithful constltu
: ents. Once also he wns sent to the Senate
:to complete an unexpired term. The most
i important part of his Congressional ca
| reer began in 1801. The Republicans had
elected Jefferson and a majority of the
; House of Representatives, and so had
j come "into power," as we use the term.
! Macon of â¢ North Carolina was chosen
I Speaker, and he appointed Randolph
' chairman of the Ways and Moans Com-
I mittee. In that position he was, as Trent
says, "one of the most efficient and thor
â  oughgoing party leaders on the floor of
' the House of Representatives that have
been produced since the birth of the na-
I tion." Later, during Jefferson's second
' term, he went Into opposition and became
the leader of a small faction called the
'â  "Quids," and thenceforth, in the words of
Trent, "was the most pertinacious and
personally dreaded free-lance in politics
1 that this country has ever known."
In ISOI the great obstacle in the way of
' the Republicans, who wished to keep the
| nation from gaining ground at the cx
i pense of the States, was the national
Judiciary, for as long: as the Federal court
i could interpret the laws of Congress and
! pass upon their constitutionality it had
the advantage in determining the relation
which national and State forces should
' bear to each other. Quite at the close of
John Adams' administration Circuit
I Courts had been organized and Judges
from the Federalist party had been ap
, pointed to rill the new places. To get rid
i of these Judges was the effort of the Re
! publicans, assisted by Randolph. It was
'- conceded that they could not be disposed
of by direct removal, nor by reduction of
I salary. The constitution too plainly for
i bade a resort to either method. But could
I not the Circuit Courts themselves be
I abolished? Such a question might have
i been expected from the Federalists, who
I stood boldly for a broad construction of
the constitution and for limiting the gen
eral Government to the exercise of those
'â â  powers alone which were plainly given to
it in the constitution. The right to abol
ish a Federal court could be found only
among those "implied powers" for using
! which the Federalists had been attacked
Iby the Republicans. But party expedi-
I ency prevailed over consistency, the Cir-
c uit Court system, in so far as it called
for a separate grade of Judges, was abol
ished by the repeal of the judiciary law
of 1801. and so the most important of
I Adams' "midnight appointees" were re
i turned to private life.
The next important administration
measure was the purchase of Louisiana,
and in supporting this Randolph went
even farther than Jefferson himself, for,
while the latter desired an amendment
to the constitution to legalize the pur
chase, Randolph considered an amend
ment unnecessary. That is to say, the
Republicans were at liberty to stretch the
constitution, although the Federalists had
been roundly denounced for doing the
Hrimo thing- while they were in power.
Furthermore, he approved of making the
purchase without any reference of the
matter to the States, although the large
increase in the number of the latter,
likely to be carved in future out of the
immense Louisiana territory, made the
purchase a matter in which the existing
Statea had the most vital interest. Hence
i in these two great mattersâ the repeal of
I the judiciary law and the purchase of
. Louisianaâ Randolph's action contradicted
his avowed principles in the matters of
State sovereignty and a limited national
â  government
Courts of the lower grades might be
abolished by act of Congress, but the
Supreme Court could not be disposed of
â  in such an offhand manner, for it came
i into being not at the discretion of Con
gress but by express mandate of the con
: Ptitution. It was thus protected by the
very principle which had been used to get
rid of the Circuit Courtâ i. c., that the
i power to create carries with it the power
,to destroy. Accordingly, unless the eon
; ptitution were changed, the only way In
! which a Supreme Court Judge couM be
' removed was by impeachment. Doubtless
| the Republicans would have been glad of
! a good excuse to impeach John Marshall.
| the great Federalist Chief Justice, whnÂ«n
decisions steadily tightened the coil of
' national supremacy around the Stato<.
i But he could not be charged with crimes
JOHN RANDOLPH.
and misdemeanors, and they were not
ready to use the serious process of im
peachment as a purely political weapon.
So they turned instead on one of his col
leagues, whose course had furnished some
basis for an attack.
Justice Chase of the Supreme Court wa3
a learned and upright Judge, who dis
charged his duties well in ordinary case: .
| But he was a violent partisan Federalist,
and permitted his party bias to be plainly
seen when matters involving party ques
| tions came before him. He thus incurred
| the hatred of the Republicans, and they
I set to work to remove him by the pr
of impeachment. The House appointed
managers, chief among whom was Ran
i dolph, and upon him fell the principal
I burden of conducting the prosecution. He
! could not have undertaken a task for
: which he was less fitted. The occasion
called for a thorough knowledge of law,
; and this Randolph did not possess. It
! also called for good judgment in deciding
j on the line of attack, and a dignified,
! forcible presentation of the case, and here
; again he was deficient. None of the arts
: of the political stump orator nor the pow
i ers of invective which he used so much
in the place of argument could serve him
now. At the bar of the Senate his au
: thority as leader of the House availed
! him nothing. Opposed to him as counsel
) for Chase were some of the foremost
! lawyers in the country, among them Lu
ther Martin, "the Federal bulldog," who
! enjoyed nothing more than exposing Ran
dolph's weaknesses. It was a rare oppor
j tunity for the Federalist champion to pay
I off old scores for his party. Randolphs
discomfiture was complete. Toward the
close of the trial he broke down, wan
i dering aimlessly in his talk, frequently
; complaining of the loss of hi? notes and
; practically throwing himself on the pity
| of those whom he had set out to instruct
I and convince. A more humiliating posi
tion could hardly be imagined for a man
of his imperious nature, accustomed to
rule and to bear down without mercy
upon those who came in his way. Judge
Chase was acquitted, the attack on the
Supreme Court failed and Randolphs
prestige received a blow from which It
did not recover.
For the sake of Randolph's reputation
it is pleasant to turn from his defeat to
his manly contest against the Vazoo
frauds. Theso constituted, in the early
part of the century, a famous political
scandal in which marly all the Legisla
ture of Georgia was implicated. Tho mat
ter at stake was the disposal of certain
great tracts of public land, in which It
turned out that the members of. the r.p-j>
lature had a pecuniary interest. Rand
exposed the scandal fearlessly. Into the
fight against corruption he threw himself
with fierce energy. When ho wished to
ruin a man politically ho would hring
against him the charge of being a "Ya
: zoo man." Indeed, in his bitter hostility
to everything "Yasoo" be wi nt too far,
for he opposed, and for a while prevented
a settlement of the matter by Conr
which aimed to protect innocent pu:
era of thÂ«* hinds. But on the whole, this
part 01 his career showed in a favorable
light his personal honesty and the high
standard of service for men in public life
Upon which he then insisted.
His constructive work aa a statesman
consisted in laying tho foundations of
Southern political supremacy in national
(iffaira. This he did by making a skillful
| use of adherrneo to Stato sovereignty and
devotion to slavery, which were already
; characteristic of his section. Altta
' there was no necessary connection be
tween the two, yet on the whole they
wnt together, both growing stronger at
the South, while they were disappearing
at the North. Randolph saw that the two
could be made to support each other and
become the basis of a controlling force,
afterward called from its most obtrusive
i element the "slave power." In building
up this power Randolph was the forerun
ner of Calhoun. who elaborated his views
j and became tho chief agent in developing
at the South that attitude toward national
affairs which led for awhile to the dom
inance of that section, but eventually to
secession and the civil war. That, as we
know, led to the destruction of sla
and the doctrine of State sovereignty and
so to the complete overthrow of that
school of statesmanship which Randolph
| did so much to establish.

Iron Is Iron Nowadays.
The Commercial News pays:
"The scarcity of pig iron in this country
has led to purchases from Scotch producers,
and it is said that small reshir>ments have
been made from China and Japan, with further
engagements of 10.000 tons to be returned from
the latter country- The Iron thus secured was
Fold to Japan two years ago, In competition
With other countries, and will cost, laid down
at San Francisco, '16 75 per ton, leaving, at
the current rate for pis Iron, a margin of
$5 25 prints on the reshipment. Any old thinK
in the way of iron 1s worth Its weight. Cuban
cannon balls have been imported in quantity
at about Jls to $17 per ton, and a full cargo
of about 3000 tons has been hipped from Cuba
to Glasgow. The Philippines are also to be
drawn on for old iron."

EASTERN MARKETS,
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Nov. The stock market took
on a show of great animation and strength to
day and some very striking net pains resulted.
The general list, however, though higher in
price at the close, developed little activity.
The operations which totaled 9.10,206 shares
Bold, were largely confined to the professional
element. The upward movement was obviously
based upon the notably easier tone of. the |
money market. The largest operations were
ny powerful pools and strong financial interests
which have command of money resources out
side of the usual money market circles. The
prevailing opinion in Wall street Is that prices
of stocks would move upward easily If the oh-
Btacle of stringent money were removed. The
large outstanding short interest In stocks has
been palpably uneasj- for some time past and j
any indication of relaxation In the tightness
of money makes them inclined to cover their
short contracts. The professional pool oper
ations in a number of prominent stocks to-day
were obviously directed against â¢ ':â¢â  bear con
tingent and with good success. It Is very
probable, however, that the large buying at
other points is based on expectation of a rise
,in prices on account of a growing Interest in
stocks.
It is the period of the year in -which, under
normal circumstances, the extra uses for
â money In the moving of the crops begin to
show diminution, releasing large funds from
employment in stock speculation. Expert opin
ion, however. Is by no meanp unanimous that
funds will be released in the usual volume this
fall, owing to the marvelous expansion In busi- j
ngs. Outside the highly speculative indus- !
trials the movement in the railroads centered In j
the Southwestern group. The. special strength l
and activity In Southern Pacific helped the i
Southwestern and was based on current ;
rumors of readjustment of control. :
The belated movement in cotton was â  factor
'of strength among Southern and Southwestern
railroads. There were special movements be
. Hides In Big Four and Che^peake and Ohio,
and some demand for the coalers.
The upward movement In the Iron and steel
Ftocks encountered difficulties hs did that In
Brooklyn Transit. Speculation in Leather con
tinued on an enormous scale, the stock rising :
an extreme 3%. Sugar was also very prominent
and rose at one time five points. There were
notable movements in Standard Rope, People's
Qua' and Manhattan. The extent of the ad
vance Invited heavy realizing, especially dur
ing the last hour and some stocks showed re
actions of between 1 and 2 points from the
highest, The general tendency was yielding.
In spite of a fresh advance In Southern Pacific
to 11% and in Chesapeake and Ohio to 23. ThÂ«
upward Impetus nt the opening was caused by
higher quotations from London, and the effect
was exaggerated by the. circulation of a canard
alleging a disastrous defeat of the Boers at
Ladysmlth. Manipulation for the rise was very
apparent all day.
The bond market was active and strong, thÂ»
demand being well distributed. Total sales,
par value, $3,395,000.
United States old 4Â« advanced *4 and the 3s
new, 4s and 6s % in the bid price.

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO, Nov. Wheat opened weak and
' showed very little recuperative power. Prices
i remained below yesterday's level throughout
! the session. Nearly everything was against
: the market and long Wheat came out In large
quantities. The principal weakening Influence
was the excess of visible stocks this year com-
I pared with last and the fact that dally ad
â¢ ditions are being made to the oversupply. At
j this time last year there were but 15,000,000
bushels in the visible, whereas now there are
40,000,000 bushels. Of this amount 20,000,000
bushels are stored in Chicago against only 3,
--000,000 a year ago. Heavy cables and the
promising condition of the winter Wheat, to
gether with an excessively slack cash demand,
encouraged the selling movement. Near the
end of the session covering by shorts started
a slight rally, but the market closed weak
within %c of the bottom. December opened
%c lower at 69 1 ,t,'569%c. sold sparingly at 69% c,
declined to 6S%e and closed with sellers at
68 7 /t,c.
Corn was active but weak with Wheat.
Light clearances, a slow cash demand and an
estimate by a prominent statistician that the
world's corn crop would amount to 2.605, 000,000,
against 2,446,000,000 bushels last year, also en
couraged selling, under which the market de
| clined steadily until late in the session, when
claims of disappointing husking returns, to
gether with covering by shorts caused some
reaction. December closed at 307b<&'31c.
Sympathy with the other grains and heavy
commission-house selling of the December op
tioned weakened OatÂ«. New estimates which

Hay and Feedstuff
There Is no further change in Feedstuffg, ex- I
cept a slight decline in Rolled Barley. Hay
continues firm for choice dry stock. The cir
cular of Somers & Co. says:
"Again we note a week of light receipts, j
even lighter than for the week preceding, the |
total amount received for the week being about
2500 tons. There Is still the cry from all sec
tions of the country for cars for the shipment
of Hay. and, no doubt, if plenty could be se
cured shipments would be heavier.
"Much of the Hay now coming in Is re- ;
ceived In a damaged condition, and is being j
worked 'iff with much difficulty and then only
at very low prices. Good Hay in a dry condi
tion Is selling fairly well, and for choice Hay
we note a very good market with prices some- j
what higher. We think that after the damaged
Hay is cleared oft the market we will
tee somewhat better prices for all grades of
Hay, although we do not expect any material
advance.
"About 600 tons of Alfalfa have arrived dur
inK the week, most of which has been received
In a damaged condition. It is very hard to dis
pose or this Hay, even at very low prices, but
sound Alfalfa Is bringing good figures. When
the damaged Alfalfa has been disposed of we
expect the market for this grade of Hay to ad
vance somewhat."

THE STOCK MARKET.
A slight advance in Giant Powder to $85 23
was the only noteworthy change In securities
on the morning session. Business was good.

than for some time and fluctuations were more
numerous. Giant Powder led in activity and
advanced from $S5 25 to $87. Gas & Electric
sold briskly at $60@53 62%. Another active
stock was Hutchinson Plantation at J2S 50@
2& 57%. Hawaiian Commercial sold at $95 ami
Hana at $10 75&11 12%. Oceanic was weaker
at $90 50.

RAISED A BILL.
Grand Jury May Charge O. Newhaus
With Fraud.
The Grand Jury yesterday considered
charges against O. Newhaus, the mining
operator, who hns had charge of several
rf the mining properties in this State of
Krnest F. Wlltsee, the millionaire mining
magnate.
Newhaus is accused of having raised a
bill of the Fulton Iron Works Company
from 1300 to $600. fraudulently obtaining
thereby the difference in money. The
jury â¢ ard several witnesses, among
them Colonel J. F. Doolittle of the St
Nicholas Hotel, and finally took the mat
ter under advisement. A special session
will be had this afternoon and the bring
ing of an Indictment against Newhaus
will be considered.
ATTACKED LUCY'S CHARACTER.
Arg-ument in Hite's Defense Made by
Attorney Foote.
W. W. Footc opened the orgument for
the defense In the Hite case before Judge
Jones yesterday morning. Mr. Foote at
tacked the character of the plaintiff, In
dian Lucy. Hiding his grill by discussing
tlio testimony of various witnesses to the
eflVct that Lucy had admitted that Hite
was not the father of her child. Charles
F. Hanlon followed Mr. Foote on behalf
of the plaintiff. He went over the Story
of how Lucy on seeing the marriage of
Hite to till present wife went before the
Superior Judge of Mendocino County and
made known the wrong done her. How
the defense nf the down-trodden woman
had been tnken up by the honest people
of the section and prosecuted to the pres
ent state of litigation. The argument will
probably be concluded to-day.

UNCONFIRMED HERE.
Reported Sale of the Stanford_South^
em Pacific Stock a Surprise.
As was the case when the sale of the
Crocker interests in the Southern Pacific
Company was first reported, the utmost
secrecy is maintained regarding the saie
of the Stanford interest.- which was re
ported yesterday in the dispatches from
New York. Mrs. Stanford is in New lorK
with her attorney. Mr. Huntingdon is out
of town, and Charles Lathrop, brother to
Mrs. Stanford, says he knows absolutely
nothing about the reported sale save that
which he bus seen In the newppapern.
The report may be true, and it it is.
Mrs. Stanford Bhould have received some
thing like SH.iKm ' â¢*Â»Â«" ''â 
stocK in the Southern Pacific i pmpany.
besides the money she would get from tne
sale of her interests in the Pacific Im
provement Company.
It was generally believed that Mrs.
Stanford had gi\en her railroad sto
the Leland Stanford Jr. University
time ago. Jn fact, the statement was
made to that effect and accepted as true
by every one. I: that was so, it is rather
hard to conceive how she could have so.d
that which she no longer possessed.
The sale of the Stanford stot k will make
but little difference in the policy of the
road. The Stanford and Crocker lnt<
combined were Btrong enough to glvi
shrewd old -entleman at the head of the
corporation all he could do to mal
his sway. But with the Crocker shares
not only out of the way but in the hands
of the enemy, Mrs. Stanford was prac
tically powerless t.. make her in!'
felt In the management of the company
in which she had so much capital in
vested.
If the stock has been purchased, it is
almost ;>. certainty thut C. P. Huntington
is the man who has bought it: and this.
together with the Crocker interests, which
are now his. and his own original hold-
Ings, makes him I i all intents and pur
poses the sole owner of the largest rail
way system in the world.

appreci itive hair dn -^ r amounts to sev

anaugh started in pursuit and overtook

OAKLAND. Nov. 2.-Chas. O. Alex
ander, Assistant Postmaster, con

ernoon by drowning in the estuary of San

The suicide was very determined, and is
particularly sad, from the fact that he
died in total ignorance of the fact that
his two most influential friends, Senator
Perkins and Congressman Metcalf, were
.'ding with the postal authorities at
Washington, while he was wallowing in
the mire, to defer any action in the mat
ter of the shortage until they should
reach Washington.
Alexander's body was found lying on
the mud ilats oft the foot of Seventeenth
avenue, East Oakland, shortly after 5
o'clock by Thomas Weingart, residing on
East Fourteenth street. Weingart had
gathering drift wood on the incom
ing tide. His little boat was loaded and
as he pulled toward the old Derby wharf
near the East Oakland tanneries he saw
a row boat adrift and near it what ap
-1 pea red to him to be a dog. Investigation
revealed the object to be the body of a
. man, and lie quickly hauled the remains
aboard his craft and rowed to the wharf.
; The Coroner was immediately summoned
te body taken to the Morgue.

Woodberry, lying in the estuary. Captain

toward the stream and opposite the
] (erby wharf. Falkman procured another
craft and secured his boat. In it he found
a hat and coat, which were late to-night

lice Department and taken to the
Morgue. But In its pockets nothing was

te touching upon the cause for the
suicide.

SOUTHERN PACIFIC HAS
ACKNOWLEDGED ERROR
OAKLAND, Nov. 2.â The Southern Pa
cific Is anxious t" make a better showing
and lias issued the following order to
trainmen:
I am inclined to believe that there is not
proper attention given as to the merchandise
that your train contains when leaving terminal
points. Also, when picking up loads, some of
you are not giving attention so that you may
know whether your engine is loaded to its ca
pacity or overloaded. On receipt of this I want
you to check up the merchandise before leaving
terminal points and note the same on your
train register. If you discover that your train
contains more merchandise than your engine
Is rated for, you will request trainmaster or
yardmaster to reduce your tra.n to rating of
your engine, providing you do not have to set
out perishable or rush cars. I ask that special
attention be given to 'his, bo that we may
bring our tonnage up and make a better show
ing than in the past. In case your engine can
not handle the train and make its time. I want
full particulars wired why.
JAMES AGLER.
Superintendent.

GRAND JURY INDICTS
BISHOP FOR ARSON
OAKLAND, Nov. 2.â The Grand Jur.
made a partial report to-day before
Superior Judpe Mall and presented an
indictment charging Robert Bisnop of
Golden Gate with arson.
(The inquisitors had spent several days
in an investigation of the affair. . rom
the testimony of Policeman Lay ton, Fire
Chief Ball and others, who appeared be
fore the grand Jurors, it was shown that
shortly after 3 o'clock on the morning: of
October 26 Bishop's house on Forty-ninth
street, near Park, at Golden Gate, was
found to be on tire. After the firemen
had succeeded in extinguishing the flames
they discovered many evidences of in
cendiarism; coal oil had been poured over
the floors of the two-story structure, and
on the walls; holes had been bored
through the ceiling of a room and oil
poured in; and the empty coal oil cans
were also found. Bishop and his son
were in rooms on the second floor, and
I while the son apparently knew nothing
i of the affair, the father claimed that the
work was that of robbers. He was ar
rested at the time, but no direct charge
was filed.
The jury had about concluded its in
quiry into the matter to-day when Bishop
arrived at the courthouse and asked an
audience. He was cheerfully accommo
dated and remained behind the closed
doors perhaps fifteen minutes. Evidently
;he made a poor Impression, for ten
i minutes later the Grand Jury fiied into
j Judge Hall's courtroom to present the
1 indictment.
Bishop's bail was fixed at $3000 and he
was turned over to the custody of the
i Sheriff. The accused is nearly GO years
! of age, and for some time was employed
j by E. G. Buswell, dealer in paints and
oils, as a driver. His son is connected
with an insurance company.

DEWEY'S WEDDING
WILL BE VERY SIMPLE
Ceremony Will Probably Be Per
formed by Cardinal Gibbons
and Archbishop Keane.
WASHINGTON. Nov. 2. â Admiral |
Dewey and Mrs. Mildred Hazen have de
cided to have their marriage take place in
tho latter part of the month, possibly be
fore Thanksgiving Day. although much
will depend upon the completion of the
bridal trousseau before the date 18 defi
nitely determined upon. Both Admiral
Dewey and Mr?. ITazen are opposed to
anything 1 versing upon display and have
informed their friends that they will be
married In the same quiet. Informal man
ner as were Rear Admiral and Mrs. Lud
lnw several years since.
Mrs. Ludlow is Mrs. Hazen's sister.
The date of their marriage was given out
only the nipht before and the ceremony
was performed In the morning. There
were no extra flowers, music or anything
of the sort, and only the immediate fam
ily witnessed the ceremony. This Is ex
actly to the liking of Admiral Dewey and
Mrs. Hazen and they have decided to fol
low the same simple ceremonial to the
letter. The ceremony will be performed In
the Empire drawing room of the mansion
(â f Mrs. 'Washington McLean, and Cardi
nal Gibbons, assisted by Archbishop
Kvano, under whose instructions Mrs.
Hazen became a Roman Catholic, will of
ficiate. If Cardinal Gibbons cannot be
present, It is said the Archbishop will tie
the nuptial knot.
Mrs. Hazen and her sister, Mrs. Lud-
Inw, left for New York yesterday after
noon, where they will remain until Mon
day. Their errand Is to select all the im
portant trousseau. Since the day of the
engagement Admiral Dewey has sent his
fiancee great bouquets of exquisite flow
ers, huge bunches of American beauty or
pink roses, as well as clusters of brilliant
yellow and pink chrysanthemums.

NEW SYMPHONY
CLUB RESUMES
WORK AT LAST
Mrs. Pheba Hearst the
Patroness.
HOLMES CHOSEN LEADER
PROF. WILLIAM CAREY JONES
IS PRESIDENT.
â â¢
A few weeks aco The Call made the
announcement that there was trouble in
the Symphony Society and that the mem
bers were highly indignant because of > ie
carelessness of the officers in m king ar
rangements for a new series of concerts.
The article further stated that in case I
nothing: Is done within a reasonable time, j
preparations will be made to organize a I
separate body, whose purpose it shall be
to commence a movement to reorganize
the symphony concerts. The meeting has
finally been held, and the following com
mittee has taken charge of affairs. It
must be understood that this commutee
is in no way connected with the old |
Symphony Society, but will direct matters j
according to its own ideas.
The committee consists of Professor
William Carey Jones of the University
of California, chairman; H. B. Pasmore,
Robert Tolmie, J. N. Odell, W. A. Sabln, !
and Mesdames J. N. Odell, A. r". Morri- â 
son. J. N. Goewey, James Tucker, C. O. ,
Richardson and J. J. Price. Mrs. Phebe
Hearst, in accordance with her usual
desire to aid beneficial movements, i.as
kindly donated the sum of $2000 for 'he
purpose of commencing the concerts.
And her main desire in acting thus
generously was to enable the new society
to defray expenses so that the musicians
may receive their price of $8 per concert,
which she claims is by right due them.
The conductor will be Henry Holmes, a
musician who has had a great deal of ex
perience in this kind of work, and i/bo
possesses exceedingly flattering tesi.
monlals from the foremost musical
authorities. He was until recently a
member of the Royal Conservatory of
Music in London, and has a splendid
record In Europe. He recently organized
a chamber music quartet in 'his city
which will begin concerts next Tuesday
at Century Club Hall.
Mrs. Hearst hopes that her donation
will create an interest among he sub
scribers to the old symphony conceits,
and spur them on to encourage a move
ment which is so essential to musical
progress in San Francisco. The .Irst
symphony concert will take place at the
Grand Opera-house on Thursday. Decem
ber 7. at 2:30 o'clock p. m. Professor
Jones, who was very prominently men
tioned for president for the University of
California a short time ago, is a man of
great executive ability, and will no doubt
prove a iplendid presiding officer. The
committee put Itself on record as an able
body of people by arranging a scale of
prices which will enable every student, bo
he every so deficient of means, to attend
them. The seating capacity of tne opera
house permits a 25-cent admission fee,
and for this purpose the family circle or
balcony has been selected. The other
prices will range as usual.
MILES IN WASHINGTON.
SEATTLE, Nov. 2.â General Nelson A.
Miles and party arrived here at noon to
day and left this evening for an inspec
tion of the new army fortifications at i
Port Townsend. He declined to discuss j
the Philippine situation further than to
say that peace depended upon circum
stances and that he would, go to the Phil
ippines if ordered. Of Puget Sound he
"This section is not only desirable but
suitable and favorably looked upon as a I
base of supplying the needs of the I nited :
States army in the Philippines. There Is |
no route that is superior for the shipment |
of both troops and animals on account
of the cooler atmosphere that prevails on
this Sound. More shipping will be done
from here and return shipments will prob
ably be made to Puget Sound at no dis
tant date. The westward posts I have

TEACHERS IN SESSION.
BAKERSFIEIvD, Nov. 2.â The third an
nual session of the San Joaquin Valley
Teachers' Association opened here to-day.
Three hundred teachers are present from
the valley counties hesirtes the teachers
of this county. Benjamin Ide Wheeler,
Hon T. J. Kirk, Harr Wapner. Joaqum
Miller and Professor E. E. Brown of
Berkeley are present as lecturers. Davtd
Starr Jordan arrives to-morrow. Mr.
AVheeler addressed a large audience to
day, creating great enthusiasm by the
strong position he took for making teach
ing a profession. He said the schools are
no place for women to rest awaiting mat
rimony, or for men hoping to get into
law or business. Teaching must be the
end and aim of teachers.

HON. HORACE DAVIS
AMONG HIS FRIENDS
Honest Workingmen Cheer the
Republic n Nominees.
The workingmen of the Twenty-eighth
District filled every inch of sitting and
standing room in the large hall at Folsom
and Second streets last night. The meet
ing was presided over by Thomas Bryant,
who in a few well-chosen words intro
duced the speakers of the evening, the
first of whom was Hon. Horace Davis.
The appearance of the next Mayor of San
Francisco was thfr-occasion for an ovation
from the hard-working men of the section
of the city where Mr. Davis had given
employment to thousunds of laborers for
a number of years and where every man
and woman in the neighborhood was
ready to vouch for kindness of heart and
liberality to such as needed his assisU
ance in the days gone by.
In the few remarks made by Mr. Davis
ho was applauded to the echo, and on his
departure from the platform he was
stepped in the body of the hall by hun
dreds of the men who knew him personal
ly and assured of their votes on next
Tuesday.
Among the Republican nominees who
made short addresses were: C. L. Nagle,
W. Watson, Judge JT. L. Joachimsen, C.
A. Low, W. '/.. Tiffany. W. A. Deane and
J. H. Scott. Alfred J. Morganstern, R.
B. McClellan and Major C. W. Kyle spoke
for the general ticket. The appearance of
Martin Kelly in the body of the hall
br< ught forth a round of cheers, which
would not down until he was forced to
ascend the rostrum, where he said: "I
am out for the Republican ticket from top
to bottom. I have been a Republican all
my life, and tho Examiner cannot drive
me out of the party. I ask you one and
nl! to vote the entire Republican ticket."
(.Cheers.)

SHOUTS AND CHEERS
FOR REPUBLICANS
Horace Davis' District Turns
Out in Large Numbers.
Plxley Hall, on the corner of Polk and
Pacific streets, was packed to the street
| last night when Chairman Brandt called
the meeting to order. It was a Repub
lican gathering of the Forty-first District,
j the club of which has for its president
Horace Davis.
No band or bonfire was needed to fill
i the hall with eager, enthusiastic voters,
yet both of these political adjuncts were
furnished. According to the programme
the candidates were to start from Pixley
Hall and then go the rounds. There was
some delay in their arrival, but the crowd
which filled every nook and cranny of the
hall manifested no Impatience and lis
tened with rapt attention to the district
orator, Sidney M. Ehrman.
When Mr. Davis arrived the enthusiasm
knew no bounds and the shout of wel
come was carried down the hall and out
on the street, where many who could not
be accommodated with seats were stafld
lng. Not for some time has there been
I such a spirited meeting in the Forty-first
and everything presages a return to the
old-time Republican majorities.

FORTIETH IS STRONG
FOR HORACE DAVIS
Spirited Meeting of Republicans
at Franklin Hall.
Franklin Hall, on Fillmore street, near
Bush, resounded with cheers for Horace
Davis last night. The citizens of the
Fortieth came early, and before the meet
ing 1 was called to order by Chairman J.
T. Williams the large hall was packed
with voters.
Men flocked from all over the district
to hear the speakers, and they did not
stint applause or enthusiasm. Edmund
Tauszky. Lucius Solomons and Myron
"Wolfe entertained the audience by bright
speeches in which campaign issues re
ceived a fair and honest consideration.
Horace Davis entered the hall later in
the evening and was cheered to the echo.
If the Fortieth District meeting last night
is a criterion there will be a Republican
sweep there next Tuesday. The candi
dates dropped in from time to time and
spoke for themselves and the ticket. The
enthusiasm, which was at no time lack
ing, was spontaneous and expressive.

A ROUSING MEETING
Horace Davis Enthusiastically
Received by a Big Audience.
. The Phoenix Republican Club held a
rousing meeting last night at Scottish
Hail. Tne spacious hall was crowded to
the doors and the enthusiasm ran high.
General A. 1... Hart presided. When Hon.
Horace Davis arrived the audience rose
and cheered him to the echo. Mr. Davis
discussed the issues of the campaign and
was heartily applauded from time to time
during his remarks.
Among the candidates who addressed
the meeting were Edwin M. Sweeney,
Charles G. Nagle and L.. G. Carpenter,
candidates for Police Judge; John Lack
mann, candidate for Sheriff; Albert
Heyer, candidate for Assessor; William
A.'Peane. candidate for County Clerk,
and Joseph 11. Scott, candidate for Tax
Collector.
PHELAN SPEAKS AT
POTRERO MEETING
Mayor Phelan spoke nt the open air
meeting held near the Union Iron Works
last night and was well received. In his
speech he told how he had visited the
camp of the enemyâ the gas company â
and found Intelligent men employed there
who coincided with him and his political
views. He said that he felt satisfied that
with the assistance of the Australian bal
lot system the laboring men's votes would
be cast for the Democratic party.
''The opposition, the Pie-publican party,
says that the Mayor will have 6000 posi
tions to fill, and they try to prove it with
I figures," said the Mayor. "I say that
I there are but thirty-three appointments
to be made, and three of these are posi
tions in the Mayor's office. While it is
true that this is a source of weakness, I
would like to have those 6000 appoint
ments. When the opposition dwells so
strongly on the matter of immense power
of the next Mayor, I think that under a
Republican administration the office
would be one of danger. It is right that
I municipal elections should be (Separated
| from national ones."
After leaving the open air meeting, the
Mayor spoke at a meeting at Eighteenth
| and Florida streets. During the Mayor's
j speech, and after a burst of applause
' which greeted a remark of Mr. Phelan
had subsided, a voice from the back of
the hall shouted, "Never mind, we'll
â  make you Senator yet!" to which the
i Mayor replied that natioual issues should
â¢ be eliminated from municipal affairs.
Mayor Phelan also spoke at the Y. M.
C. A. gymnasium at Hill and Valencia
streets.
PANHANDLE PROJECT
MEANS CONFISCATION
Continental League Voices the
Protests of Citizens.
The Continental League of Republican
Clubs, voicing the opinions of hundreds of
voters and property holders along the line
and within reach of the results of Mayor
Phelan's panhandle scheme, have drafted
and adopted the following resolutions:
Whereas.' Lnet year Hon. James D. Fhelan
was a gold bug. this year a conundrum, some
kind of a Democrat, partly gold, partly silver,
a mixture of a non-partisan, a clalmer of Re
publican Ideas and measures -while fishing for
Republican votes; and
Whereas. Mayor Phelan. with an eye to wind
ward looking for the main chance recommends
the Issuance of $10,000,000 worth of bonds by the
people to continue the park panhandle, and in
our opinion the immediate extension of the
park panhandle to the Intersection of Market
street and Van N'ees avenue will materially
enhance the value of his large holdings of real
estate on Market Btreet cunt of the proposed
terminus of said park panhandle, while It will
throw a cold froet over the property of the peo
ple on Market street west of the proposed pan
handle and thÂ« entire Mission belt; therefor*
Veaolved. That we, the members of the Con
tinental League, will work for and vote for
Hon Horace David for Mayor because he is
a statesman, a stalwart Republican, a manu
facturer and employer of worklngmen; because
he favors a bond issue for the following needed
public Improvements:
Firstâ Needed hospitals for the care and com
fort of sick patients. â â¢- â¢:.'â ..
Secondâ Needed new sehoolhouses and large
yards and the Improvement of the sanitary and
other conditions of the old ones. .
Thirdâ New and Improved pavements on our
public streets, kept clean, and the abandonment
of the cobblestones.
Fourthâ Needed new, complete and effective
sewer system. â 
Fifthâ A salt water plant for the purpose of
extinguishing tires, flushing and purifying the
new sewer system, without which the system
would be of little value from a sanitary point
of view. .
Sixthâ The purchase of the garbage cre
matory and allowing the garbage gatherers to
dump their garbage free of cost, thereby lessen
ing lite expense to rent payers, nnd before It
waters its stock, floats it among the people and
charges the municipality five times Its value,
which Infant corporation has been nursed and
allowed to grow under Mayor Phelan's admin
istration, to enrich a few non-partisans favor
able to his nomination and election.
Beventhâ Purchasing or building other public
utilities, such as water, eaa, etc., whenever

advantage and betterment of the city, j

Campaign Incidents.
There was a meeting of the John Laek
mann Club held at Pythian Castle last
nipht; and reports were received from
every district from the Twenty-eighth to
the 'Forty-fifth, inclusive. The reports
were all favorable, and good work is be
ing done by the club, not only for Mr.
l.ackmann, but for the whole -Republican
ticket.
The John Lackmann Club of Court De
fiance, Foresters of America, held a meet
ing in the Alcazar building that was a;
tended by a lance number of the members
of that order. The principal speaker was
the Republican candidate for Sheriff, who
is himself a Forester, and he whs followed
by a number of prominent members of
American Forestry, who gave their un
qualified support for their brother For
ester and declared that all of the 6000
American Foresters, irrespective of party
politics, will on Tuesday next cast their
votes for John Lackrr.ann.
The William Z. Tiffany Club met last
evening at Its quarters. The executive
committee reported that full arrange
ments had been made in every Assembly
1 â¢istrict to make an aggressive tight for
Mr. Tiffany from now on. Mr. Tiffany
was introduced, and made a spirited ad
dress, during which he stated that if
elected his first duty would be to inau
gurate much-needed reforms and to con
duct the affairs of the office on strictly
business principles. Mr. Tiffany was
much applauded at the close of his ad
dress. Hon. E. I. Sheehan and Hon. F.
W. Burnett also spoke in advocacy of Mr.
Tiffany's election.
Horace Davis will address a meeting at
the I'nion League Club on Saturday after
noon at 4 o'clock. Other good speakers
are invited to attend and a special invita
tion is extended to merchants to attend.

THE TIGER HAS RETURNED TO TOWN AND UNDER JOE
HARVEY'S PATRONAGE HAS RESUMED HIS SWAY

der Protection of
State Laws,
r*
Police Said to Be Unable
to Prevent the New
Outrage.
â Â« â
A Mason-Street Establishment
Incorporated as a Legal
ized Den of Crooks.
GAMBLING Is again a recognized
industry of s.iti Francisco. Not
verj long ngo Chief of Police Lees
and Police Commissioner Gunst
quarreled over the expediency of
throwing the Town "wide open" and per
mitting the gamblers to ply their truffle
at least unmolested if not under thr
actunl protection of the police. The Chief
of Police objected then, but now his ob
jections have been silenced and his threat
ening haiiil? i.:ivi- been tied. He has seen
a new and dangerous gambling house
established, He knows that under the
protection of certain State laws other
laws nnÂ» viHnted and that on one of the
mopt prominent thoroughfares of the city
a gambling house is bein^ conducted ut
terly without molestation. Its cappers
are abroad on every avenue inviting
trade. Its games are run "wide open"
end unsophisticated patrons may hazard
and l..se their money .in any game that
strikes their fancy. Faro, roulette, per
centage poker, craps and other sanies
called by courtesy games of chance are
running in full t
Visiting: horsemen, returning and com
ing soldiers, civilians. countrymen
and resident gullibles are victims of
the new robbery, and the police can nnlv
Bhrug their shoulders and ask an inquirer
mrhat they shall do. The history of the
new gambling venture is an interesting
one. Several months ago The Call ex
posed the fact thai a coterie of gamblers
Were conducting a Rambling ouirn in the
Palace Hotel. The proprietors wore reap
ing a rich harvest of ill-gotten gain. Men
of wealth and position were their patrons.
Their rooms were crowded nightly by men
â¢who had money to lose, and lost it cheer
fully.
The police were thoroughly informed of
the existence of the gambling house, but
Ihey saw fit to do nothing. The offense
against the laws was committed nightly
under their very eyes, but they saw noth
ing and did nothing. Joe Harvey, the
â wf-11-kno-wn gambler, was the master
spirit of the affair, and gossips said ritrhT
ly that he was a close and personal
friend of the Chief of Police and would
not be molested. When The Call made its
expose there, was a scampering for dark
ness and the Palace Hotel gambling house
became a lamented memory.
For pome time after its collapse no ef
iort was made to revive it. There was
trouble in the Police Commission. The
Commissioners and Chief Lees had trou
bles of their own, and the gamblers who
relied upon the good â will of the Chief
did not care to interject themselves at po
critical a Juncture. They decided to await
developments and to watch an opportun
ity to Invade again the local field. Their
lawyers delved deeply Into the law and
strove in many ways to discover a mean?
by which through the law they could
â cheat the laws. At last the avenue was
opened and the gamblers lost no time in
accepting its advantages.
The gamblers received their inspiration

PHELAN'S TALES
QUEERED BY HIS
OWN WITNESSES
â¦
Grand Jury Vindi
cates Supervisors.
Another of the cheap political tricks
with which Mayor Phelan li;is character
ized his present campaign has fallen of
its own weight, and fallen hard. A com
mittee appointed yesterday by the Grand
Jury will call upon him to-day or to
morrow to apprise him of that fact. The
charges of wholesale fraud and knavery
recently brought by his Honor against
the Hospital Committee of the Board of
Supervisors have proved so utterly
groundless that the Jury has thrown the
whole case out of its calendar and
turned In disgust to matters of more
merit.
When a few weeks ago it became appar
ent that the City and County Hospital
could not be maintained upon the
amount set aside for It In. the tax sched
ule submitted by the Mayor, and that un
less more funds were immediately forth
coming it would have to close its doors
to the indigent sick of the city, his
Honor attempted to shirk all responsibil
ity for that deplorable condition and at
the same time make a bit of cheap politi
cal capital by setting up a cry of fraud.
He sought Ills typewriter and framed
charges of jobbery and extravagance
against the Hospital Committee, and he
set it forth clearly that if there had been
no jobbery the hospital could be run upon
the amount he had allowed It. These
statements, together with a list of wit
nesses, whom he sufgesied, would be com-

RECKLESSNESS OF A BOY
COST HIM HIS LIFE
HERCULES SCHWARTZ, a 14-year-old errand boy, employed by the
Wagner Leather Company at 306 Clay street, lost his life yesterday
morning while attempting to alight from a freight elevator In the build
ing at 27 Jessie street, occupied by the KaHt-Glanville Shoe Company.
Willie Miley of 621 Girard street was in char X( > of the elevator. He said
that young Schwartz, a stranger to him, came to the elevator door very early
in the day and asked whether he was going up with the elevator. Willie re
plled that he would start in a few moments, and went after a can of oil. As
soon as he had turned his back Hercules got upon the elevator and started
up. A moment or two later Miley was horrified by the sight of Schwartz's
body falling down the elevator shaft.
atiley gave the alarm and the body of the unfortunate boy was taken out
of the well and carried to the Harbor Emergency Hospital. He was uncon
scious, and shortly after the surgeons had shaved his head in order to dress
a fracture of the skull he expired. Young Schwartz's destination was the
third floor, and it is surmised that ho attempted to jump off the cage, not
knowing how to etop it, and fell down the shaft.
He was an industrious boy and was residing with his parents at 305 Eddy
street. Three weeks ago he went to work for the Wagner Leather Com
pany.
Jabbering Chinese were sent scampering

games were broken up. although the po

placards heralded the fact thru the raided

framed certificates of incorporation gave

With consummate cunning the Chinese

to violate, other laws without molesta
tion.
After the raids the Chinese showed their
teeth. They resented the interference of
their traffic, and Patrick Crowley, who
was then chief of Police, was sued for
heavy damages. The case Is still pending
In court, but lawyers who have reason to
be well informed express the opinion that
the Chinese will win and that the law
which gives to social anil fraternal so
cieties the right of incorporation protects
them from police interference.

When the gamblers of the Palace Ho
tel found themselves without a scene for
their operations they looked around for a
new field or a new subterfuge for playing
again in the local field. Joe Harvey was,
the master spirit of the combination. He
had behind him the moral and, if neces
sary, a more substantial support from
Charley Fair. He enjoyed the friendship
of Chief of Police Lees. He was favored
with the patronage of local gamblers and
was assured of the trade of horsemen,
blacklegs and roustabouts of the race
track. The racing season was approach
ing. If money was to be made it must
be made quickly.
The town was flooded with soldiers com
ing and going. Harvey racked his
brains for an opening. He had with him
Charley Asher, the factotum and sub
servient agent of Moses Gunst, Police
Commissioner. Max Katzenstein, another
of the old Baldwin Hotel contingent, was
ready for any duty, but Harvey could not
see, even under the favored condition of
police connivance, an opportunity to open
a new gambling house.
After a deal of trouble and discussion,
the success of the Chinese offered a sug
gestion. Harvey decided to establish a
social club, and under the shadow of a
law which was Intended as a boon to
worthy institutions prostitute the laws of
