SAN FRANCISCO
TO BE SHORN OF
MUCH SHIPPING
Pacific Coast Com
pany Will Go to
Seattle.
On January 1 S;in Francisco will cease
to be the shipping -and general business
center of the Pacific Coast Steamship
Company, and all of the local interests of
that concern will be moved to Seattle.
which will thereafter be the home of the
corporation and scene of its greatest
activity.
Although no public announcement of the
fact has yet been made, It became known
on the street yesterday that Goodall, Per
kins & Co., which firm has been for years
the company's agents and managers, had
been deprived of the agency and was clos
ing up its books as rapidly as possible In
order that the formal transfer of the busi
ness might take place on or before the
appointed date. The change is due to the
fact that the Great Northern Railway
Company has secured a controlling inter
est In the Pacific Coast Steamship Com
pany. The headquarters of the railroad
are at Seattle |
By this latest deal In steamship circles
San Francisco merchants will lose all the
advantages and profits of one of the most
profitable shipping concerns on the Pacific
Coast, nave those that will accrue to the
city as one of the ports to be touched at
by the company's boats. The handsome
steamers Corona and Pomona will no
longer make this city their home port, and
the lines to Vancouver and Alaskan ports,
as well as those to Ensenada, in peninsu
lar California, and Santa Rosalia, In Mex
ico, will make the Sound City the distrib
uting point for the thousands of tons of
i means Harvey's own. An old-time gam
; bier, Fred Merker. conceived it, and would
i have made it practicable with profit for
i himself were it not for the fact that the
Harvey combination possessed more
money. Harvey and his friends seized the
suggestion and looked for a suitable lo
cation for a club.
The gamblers finally settled upon the
j house at 143 Mason street and a week
ago the gambling den was opened. It is
perhaps the worst house of its kind that
was ever opened In San Francisco. Its
cappers are abroad upon the streets in
viting the unwary to visit it. Its agents
are in the tiubs, on the streets and in
saloons advancing its interests. Its games
of faro, poker, roulette, wheel of fortune
and craps are conducted without inter
ruption. It exists as a menace not only
to people of the city, but to visitors and
strangers who. have the right to expect
police protection.
Harvey was altogether too clever to per
mit his own name to be known in the
combination. Alter consultation with his
friends, among whom was Chief of Police
Lees, Harvey secured a lease of the
premises at 143 Mason street. The con
ference with Lees took place at Farley's
saloon and Harvey was assured that the
police are powerless to act. The gambler
then lost no time in perfecting his plans.
The Mason street house was renovated
from top to bottom. It was superbly
furnished with every possible incident to
luxurious surroundings. Word went forth
that It was to be the most exclusive
gambling house ever established on the
coast and particular stress was laid upon
the fact that the police, with convenient
ignorance, would not know that It was
in existence.
On October IS a strange document was
filed with the County Clerk. It purported
to be the articles of incorporation of the
California Club. The California Club, so
the articles read, was organized for purely
social purposes, for the entertainment of
Its members and for the promotion of
their social intercourse. The document
read, with delightful untruth, that an
election had been held and the following
had been elected directors for a period of
one year: A. P. Culver, W. H. Roach,
Ray Mead, W. S. Ferguson and J. F.
Powers. The writer of the document must
have been seized with an after thought
for he scratched out the name of Roach
and substituted H. W. Orear.
These directors are not particularly well
known. Orear has the honor of being the
dispenser of cigars at the Union ferry
depot and J. F. Powers is his clerk. Raj-
Mead, as far as the directory gives in
formation, may be a vaquero, and he may
not be. The other directors have r.ot
had the consideration to give the directory
freight they now land in this city. Al
though it is known that some of the large
force of clerks and other employes now
employed in this city by Goodall, Perkins
& Co. will be taken to Seattle, a greater
number of them, it is understood, will be
thrown out of their positions on the first
of the year.
MRS. GLEASON MISSING.
Had a Quarrel With Her Husband
and Disappeared at 10:30
O'Clock at Night.
Mrs. J. A. Crounse of 12 Oak street re
ported to the Coroner yesterday that Mrs.
Lizzie Gleason of 415 Clayton street had
disappeared at half-past 10 o'clock last
Wednesday night, and ihat her relatives
feared that she had committed suicide or
met with foul play. Mrs. Crounse said
that Mrs. Gleason had a quarrel with her
husband, and that Mrs. Uteason's mother
parted from her at half-past 10 o'clock in
Scottish Hall on Larkin strefet. That was
the last time that she was seen by any of
her relatives.
The missing woman was dressed In
black, with a short jacket, and tan or
chameleon waist, a Gaincsborogfc hat with
red flowers and black plumes. Shr Is 5
feet 5 1 *: inches tall and weighs 140 pounds.
John A. Russell Improving.
John A. Russell, the clerk of the Board
of Supervisors, who suffered a stroke of
paralysis last Wednesday afternoon, is
rapidly approaching recovery in his
apartments at the Lick House. Mr. Rus
sell Is still a very sick man and needs
careful nursing and constant attention,
but his condition has so materially Im
proved that those in attendance feel con
fident that he will pull through all right,
notwithstanding his advanced age.
This news will be a source of great sat
isfaction to the many warm friends Mr.
Russell has, not only in this city but
throughout the enure coast.

This gambling club has been organized
to run for thirty years. A copy
of its articles of incorporation
has been filed with the Sec
retary of State. The rooms of the club
are open for business. Its patrons are
already numerous. Its walls echo to
the clink of coin and the sound of many
excited voices. It permits no one not a
member within its doors, but everybody
is welcome as a member. One need but
ring the doorbell, hand $5 to the negro
attendant rind receive the mock ceremony
of initiation. In consideration for this
$5 in gambling checks are given by the
club.
Plainly speaking, the club is an outrage
upon the city. It is a menace to young
men and strangers, but it is unknown to
the police aftrr the conference in Farley's
saloon. Tf the police, however, are un
able to locate 143 Mason street, and are
powerless to place an officer at the faro
table. The Call will show them the house
and instruct them how to get in.
MARKET STREET
MUST BE SANDED
TO SAVE HORSES
Wet Pavements Traps
for Drivers.
It is not uncommon in the early morning
to see half a dozen horsiis lying on the
pavement helpless in the space of two
blocks on Market street. A slight mois
ture or a fog makes the pavement almost
as slippery as glass to the feet of horses
not shod with rubber. Hundreds of driv
ers avoid the bituminized pavement, be
ing actuated by fear for their horses. So
many accidents take place that the need
of some public action is evident. The Call
this morning offers the suggestion that
Market street should be sprinkled with
-sand whenever it is necessary to make
the pavement safer to drive on. Yester
day many persons who have a wide
knowledge of horses and are largely con
cerned in the use of them were inter
viewed concerning the necessity of using
sand freely. One and all declared that the
remedy needed to improve the lot of
drivers had been discovered by The Call.
Secretary Charles Holbrook of the So
ciety for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Animals was enthusiastic for the use of
sand. He had seen six horses lying upon
the pavement in one Market street block.
The only way that the animals could rise
was by having a blanket thrown down to
give them a chance to get a footing. They
would try repeatedly to get upon their
feet unavailingly. Much time would be
lost, much unnecessary suffering inflicted
upon the horses by the repeated falls fol
lowing their struggles to get up. It had
been necessary to kill some horses because
of such accidents on Market street.
No less emphatic in indorsement of lac
plan was "W. H. Fuller of the Overland
Freight and Transfer Company. "Sand
would save the lives of many horses,"
said Mr. Fuller. "Every morning, he
teen Third street and the Palace Hotel,
I see three or four horses lying down on
the pavement. All sorts of business men
are seriously inconvenienced. You see,
when the pavements are wet there is
nothing for the horses' feet to take hnM
upon. I am heartily in favor of vie plan
suggested by The Call and it will be very
popular with all owners of horses in the
city."
John Peterson, foreman for McNab &
Smith, draymen, said: "Sanding MarKet
street when it is wet is just what ought
to be done. There is plenty of sand at
the beach and it ought not to cost much
for carting in. I know all about the
danger and inconvenience of the pave
ment -\\nen It is damp. I live out on
Octavia street and comÂ© down Golden
Gate avenue and Market street early every
morning. Some days I have to get off
the street to be safe. We lost one horse
that fell when the pavement was slip
pery."
Thomas F. Noonan is the president of
the Draymen and Teamsters' Union,
which has a membership of 175 and em
braces very large concerns. "We all con
sider Market street very dangerous,"
said Mr. Noonan, "when it is wet and
when it Is only a little damp. One valu
able horse, to my knowledge, injured by
slipping on Market street, had to be shot.
The Call has struck a good idea in sand-
J.'w. Murphy, proprietor of St. George's
stables, on Bush street, said: "Sand is the
only possible remedy for the slippery
footing on the Market street pavement
when it is wet." -
J H. Leary of the Olympic Club sta
bles, 503 Sutter, said: "Sanding would be
a very good thing, for it would let the
horses' get a foothold. By all means let
us have the pavement sanded when it is
most needed."
W. Garhart, foreman of the St. Law
rence stables, said: "The idea is a first
class one. The horses can't stand with
out having something done. Sanding is
the proper act. One of the valuable
horses stabled here fell and was injured.
I saw four horses down on one block this
Alex McCord of the Fashion stables
said: "I saw four or five horses down on
the pavement on one block to-day. I
heartily indorse the idea of The Call
about the use of sand. It will do the
business." . Â
Sand is employed successfully to make
pavements safer in London. There is no
reason why it should not do well here.
ELIZABETH MUIRHEAD
IN HER OWN DEFENSE
She Supported the Deceased and His
Child During the Last Months
of Their Lives.
The contest of the -will of the late
Charles Walter Willey was continued be
fore. Judge Troutt yesterday morning,
with Mrs. Elizabeth Mulrhead, the propo
nent, on the witness stand. By way of in
troduction to her testimony Mrs. Muir
head stated the facts of her meeting with
Willey; how she had picked him up
the street and cared for him and his puta
tive child for years prior to his death.
Regarding the will Mrs. Muirhead said
that she was given notice of the existence
of the will by Mrs. T. J. Lyons, the execu
trix, a month before Willey's death, but
phe did not open the envelope containing
the instrument until after Wllley's death.
She denied that she ever persuade! Mr.
Lyons to procure a will from Willey In
her favor, as alleged by the contestants,
and absolutely contradicted the testimony
given by Lyons regarding those matters.
Mrs. Muirhead further denied that she
had accused Willey of being the father of
an illegitimate child, but said that after
Willey was forped to recognize his child
she adopted it, paying for its mainte
nance and physicians' bills until its death
at the age of sixteen months. She also
testified as to having paid for Willey'9
clothes, laundry bills and necessaries of
life, and paid for the desk he used after
he entered the employ of Lyons.
Rabbi Nieto to Lecture.
The season of winter lectures at the
synagogue of the Congregation Shereth
Israel, corner of Post and Taylor streets,
will be inaugurated this evening, when
Rabbi Nieto will deliver an address enti
tled "Old Pictures in New Frames." The
public la cordially Invited.
Convicted of Manslaughter.
The jury in the case of Manuel Esqueda),
charged with the murder of his wife, Sol
edad Esqueda, returned a verdict of man
slaughter last night in Judge Lawlor's
court after several hours of deliberation.
